```
It can do this (along with a lot of other stuff pertaining to COBOL Source,

support if required. (Maybe negotiable for CLC people...<G>)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Searching COBOL source

- **From:** "Ira D. Baxter" <idbaxter@semdesigns.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-01T16:49:45-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3b91592d$1_1@feed1.realtime.net>`
- **References:** `<3B901BC6.DF0BD112@home.com>`

```
Our DMS Software Reengineering Toolkit is capable
of parsing and building compiler data structures for
COBOL programs,  carrying out arbitrary
transforms on the COBOL program source,
and then prettyprinting the revised source text.
Transforms are specified using patterns:
          LHS -> RHS
where LHS and RHS are arbitrary COBOL fragments with parameters.
Finding and extracting particular bits of code
would be a straightforward application.
See http://www.semdesigns.com/Products/DMS/DMSToolkit.html.
```

```
Thank you. I'm glad it was of use. (Removes yellow robe and ping-pong ball
eyes, rolls up prayer mat, pours a large Jack Daniels, and switches on
TV...)

I have had a couple of private mails already from people who saw the page
and were surprised that COBOL could be used in this way.

The important thing here is that the general perception of COBOL should be
influenced wherever possible, in a positive way.

Posters to  CLC already know the value of the language; it is the rest of
the Programming Community that we need to reach...

The Editor of the site has offered to post future articles from me and I
have agreed to provide material which could be of interest to his
readership, once .NET COBOL becomes available.

I am currently looking at COBOL server side code for my own purposes (I
believe Java Servlets meet the requirement very well, but there may be cases
where COBOL CGI code could be more appropriate, especially where RDBs are
being manipulated). Sometime in the next few months I expect to learn ASP
(my holiday here in NZ is now extended until April so I'll have some long
summer days lying on the beach and this time can be usefully employed in
furthering my education...<G>)

Eventually, with a handle on server side code, ASP and .NET COBOL, it should
be possible to contribute something useful to his site. Maybe we can even
get some people to recognise that there is a place for COBOL in the Brave
New World...

Sure hope so...

Pete.
```

- **Date:** 2001-09-05T10:10:13-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** bit.listserv.ibm-main,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com>`

```
How many copies of MVS sell each year and how many copies of Windows?

Even based on $ volume, how much revenue does MVS generate per year and how
much does Windows generate per year.

Still no contest.

The only place MVS/CICS etc. wins is in number of transactions processed.
Last time I saw numbers, CICS transactions per day worldwide still exceeded
even the number of web page views worldwide (all estimated by "gurus" of
course - nobody actually counts this stuff).
But since IBM doesn't get paid by the transaction, it doesn't matter.
And have you noticed that Bill is driving .net towards a model where he DOES
get paid by the transaction?  Cleaver boy that Bill!

Bill's strength is not now, nor has it EVER been his or M$'s technical
abilities.  Those people are Marketing geniuses.  And what always peeves us
technical types is when great marketing strategy and execution triumphs over
great technical design and coding.  That, I propose, is why we really love
to hate M$.

IBM rose to the top of the Mainframe world because in that world they were
the Marketing Geniuses.  And they used to be hated for it too.  They used
every trick in the book - like pre-announcing systems that they hadn't even
designed yet, to freeze the market and lock out compeitors (a trick the
Feds, sued and stopped them on eventually).

But it took IBM too long to make the mental leap from marketing big iron to
little iron and Microsoft passed them in the desktop world, never to be
caught.  And now that Desktop world has moved into file servers with a
vengance and is rapidly moving into large systems.

Have you seen some of the newest Unisys "Mainframes?"  32 wide Intel Itanium
processors with shared memory bus running Win2000.  A fraction of the cost
and maintenance expense for the same or better power as Unisys current
"mainframes".  And you don't even have to reboot them anymore, every time
you install new software :-).
```

```
He _would_ get paid if anyone fell for this.  Not everything MS does is
successful, there is a long trail of failures, but they tend to be
'forgotten'.

> Bill's strength is not now, nor has it EVER been his or M$'s technical
> abilities.  Those people are Marketing geniuses.  

Bill's strength is actually his litigation.  MS maintain a horde of
lawyers (originally run by Bill's father) that intimidates both
competitors and customers.

The current round of 'audits' is hardly a 'marketing triumph', but it
brings in the loot by threats and bullying.  It appears to be stepping
up so that an alternate to the audit can be had by buying all new XP
licences (which expire in 3 years) for every seat (regardless of whether
there is a computer there even).

> Have you seen some of the newest Unisys "Mainframes?"  32 wide Intel Itanium
> processors with shared memory bus running Win2000. 

Windows on Itanium is either 32 bit wastage or alpha 64bit versions. 
32bit Win2000 runs _slower_ on Itanium than on same MHz pentium. 64bit
will be talked about by MS but is just vapourware, and will not be fully
64bit (cf Win95 as '32bit'). It will be some time before this is a
viable product, and the customers know it and are staying away in
droves.  

> A fraction of the cost and maintenance expense 

For the hardware, yes.  For the software it will cost an arm and a leg
in the _per CPU_ licencing and CAL licences.  Unisys have been conned by
MS, they will make a single digit margin on the hardware while passing
most of the system total price onto MS, and then having to do the
support.  IBM saw the trend, Compaq has suffered from thinning profits
while supporting MS revenues, HP has actually noticed they made a
mistake in promoting Windows servers.

> for the same or better power as Unisys current "mainframes".  

Much better 'power', much less _performance_.

> And you don't even have to reboot them anymore, every time
> you install new software :-).

For many years Novell has not had to be rebooted when installing new
operating system patches or even complete new versions of the OS.

Now MS make a 'huge advance' in not having to reboot when an application
is installed ?
```

```
availability.

week
change, saved $100,000). This isn't bragging, it is just to indicate that
some of the
"pure mainframe" data capture jobs have potential for LOTS of financial
savings.

transaction. That is not to say that you CAN'T use M$ products, or that the
intranet isn't sometimes better.

non-bootable
Win-95 CD & a WIN-98 upgrade CD. Nowhere in the Win-98 documentation did it
suggest I could boot straight from the upgrade to install it. It was just
something that
seemed worth trying.

Then there's the copy of MS Works I have that I use to upgrade to Access-95.
Only,
the Works/Access upgrade doesn't work once the HD got too big. And have you
tried getting support from M$ ? I now have $100 of useless software.
```

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Anne & Lynn Wheeler <lynn@garlic.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-06T19:33:16+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** bit.listserv.ibm-main,comp.lang.cobol,alt.folklore.computers
- **Message-ID:** `<u1ylkcdyv.fsf@earthlink.net>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <yjNl7.6673$4z.26842@www.newsranger.com>`

```
Charles <nospam@newsranger.com> writes:

> I think the real strength of microsoft is you can buy a PC and install a program
> or an application with very little knowledge about Hardware, files or memory
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> going on here not necessarily direct competition.

In the early '80s before Apple Mac was announced, I would periodically
have dinner with some of the Mac developers. We would have this
argument about whether the Mac (then) was ever going to be
profitable. I would somewhat characterize the Mac group at targeting
the box for the kitchen table and they found the idea that it might
ever be used in any other environment (especially any sort of business
context) as an anathema.

My counter-argument was that the vast majority of the IBM/PC sales
were into business (i.e. for a whole slew of reasons, personal
computing wasn't really ready from the general, novice consumer
market) .... possibly by a ratio of 10:1. A big factor in that was
having mainframe terminal emulation on the PC ... so there was a
single desk footprint ... aka with a single screen, keyboard, box, a
person could both do local computing as well as do their mainframe
accesses (aka a big factor in early IBM/PC market penetration was that
it had at least some local computing capability and also could act as
an ibm mainframe terminal). Being able to establish a (then) huge
install base on the basis of single keyboard on the desk (capable of
both mainframe & local computing) .. that created critical mass for
motivating more applications to be written for the IBM/PC ... which
started the snowball effect; once the initial base was established,
then more applications were written, which increased the value
proposition for having a PC, leading to further increases in the
install base, motivating even more application development.

This characteristic allowed some graceful transition from mainframe
centric operation to incremental local applications. The problem by
the late '80s was the migration was starting to accelerate for both
processor-centric applications as well as data-centric applications.

This was creating a horrible bind in corporate america ... the local
computing processing power was a transient type of activity ... but
when some of the corporate jewels (like finance data) started
migrating to PCs & PC disks there were some serious episodes of disk
failure and lost data that seriously impacted business operations. 

At the same time, there was an internal corporate battle going on.
The disk storage division generated a report that the head of the
communication division was going to be personally responsible for the
demise of the disk storage division. The 2nd level detail was that the
disk storage division had come up with a number of very effective
solutions that supported PC accessing mainframe resident data. The
communication division was non-concurring on all such offerings
... effectively attempting to maintain that all PC mainframe access
had to be channeled thru the communication division's terminal
(emulation) products. The lack of bandwidth and function of the
communication division's terminal (emulation) was accelerating the
migration of data off the mainframe. That migration was also
significantly increasing the risks & business continuity exposures of
corporate american (since critical corporate assets were now resident
on platforms that lacked any business continuity &/or disaster
recovery scenerios).

Siding with the communication divison was the mainframe processor SAA
strategy which could be interpreted as 1) all application could
executed at either the mainframe or the PC ... and once that was
achieved 2) some of the mainframe->PC processor migration could be
reversed. The shortcoming was that there was some really valid reasons
for much of the mainframe->PC processing migration; but an optimal
business strategy would be to do it in such a way that critical
business assets (aka information & data) could continue to reside at
the mainframe.

The shortcoming is that once the PC began using the terminal emulation
interface as a graceful migration & market penetration ... it later
became a nearly impossible (internal) business problem to offer
something that would take business away from the division responsible
for terminal emulation (even if that limitation eventually resulted in
significant, overall loss of business to the company).

From corporate america standpoint, I believe in the mid-90s there was
some study that said that companies that had (non-backed up) disk
failures with critical corporate assets, 50 percent went bankrupt
within the first month (corporate assets could be billing file,
accounts receivable, etc ... which could seriously impact a month's
cash flow).
```

- **Date:** 2001-09-07T11:54:43-04:00

- **Message-ID:** `<9naqlp$9tc$1@allday.news.cais.net>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net>`

```
I think everybody missed what I was trying to say.
Let me try again -
What is a "Mainframe"
How do we define that term?
If it is by processing power (MIPS or some other arbitrary measure of power)
then a 2ghz Intel based PC will run rings around many of yesterday's
Mainframes.  And some of Unisys's 32 and 64 wide Intel based "mainframes"
keep up with the best of em.
If it is by the capibilities of Operating Systems?  Native transaction
processing built into the OS for example?  Then Unisys is running their
Mainframe OS on top of Windows already.

My point was - The line between "Mainframe" and "PC" is bluring very
quickly.  I don't care if it's MVS/Windows/Unix or some OS yet to be
invented, the cost/size/technology involved is all mushing together.
And what will drive existing "big iron" users to smaller systems based on
RISC or Intel Processors is pure cost.  Cost of the iron and mostly cost of
the maintenance fees.

All the issues about security and transaction processing and reliability
that were the bulwark of the Mainframe world are being solved and addressed
in lower cost systems.

In the past year I' ve been involved in several large projects to move
entire COBOL systems off "Mainframes" and onto UNIX based open systems.
They work.  They work well.  And in both cases, just a single years savings
in hardware/software maintenance and licensing paid for the cost of moving
and modernizing the code and interface.

There was a time when IS managers assumed that moving code off a Mainframe
meant recoding in something other than COBOL.  No longer - now we are simply
moving COBOL systems to newer systems.

My point in talking about the relative sizes/revenues etc. between MVS and
M$ is to point out how the gaps are all closing or have been passed.

COBOL will survive - but hardware and operating environments will change -
its inevitable.  Moore's law dictates it.
```

```
in the late '80s ... after something like 20 years in the mainframe

platforms (she had done stint in POK in charge of loosely-coupled

proponents of clusters were some of staunchest advisaries at the time.

survivability (terms we had coined). we got a chance to author
sections of the corporate continuous availability strategy document
... but both rochester & POK non-concurred ... and most of our stuff
was pulled from the document.

in the late '80s there was big government push in the HDTV area
... the commerce department essentially taking the position if non-US
corporations dominated HDTV area ... that the associated technology
volumes would allow them to then dominate all aspects of electronics
hi-tech industry.

some random HDTV refs:

http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001b.html#2 FCC rulemakings on HDTV

Effectively, the volumes associated with consumer electronics provided
sufficient R&D funding that they could pass the pure hitech computer
industry ... aka the volumes could drive design of better, faster, &
less expensive chips.

There were some issues with quality ... but once the volumes got large
enuf, many areas started finding that it was cheaper to put a lot of
upfront costs into quality than the costs of the associated trouble
calls & returns (commodity priced disks going from MTBF of 40k hours
to 800k hours). In the disk area, if RAID was then layered on top, it
really became a no-contest.

This carried over into the software arenea, for instance could
(relative) low-volume operating systems maintain the new R&D
technology investments that was going into the high-volume operating
systems tracking newest technology advances.

In that sense, at some point a threshold was passed ... and the big
iron environments had to start playing catchup (they still had their
legacy apps of 30+ years). Some issue is does the legacy apps
represent sufficient value and revenue flow to underwrite the
necessary new R&D investment to keep abreast of what is going on in
some of the higher volume market segments.

Amdahl gave a seminar at MIT around '71 analysing what part
360s would play in the marketplace (the audience did somewhat roast
him for being a front for foreign interests based on the Amdahl corp
business plan/funding). It included some observation about the amount
of software investment in the legacy applications and the, then,
projected costs of rewriting applications for a new or different
platform ... which wasn't likely to happen any time in the next 30
years (it has now been 30 years). Part of the issue is that there has
been some advances in software technology ... and there are some now
standardized product offerings that offer the feature/function of the
roll-your-own, ground-up legacy implementations (potentially
significantly mitigating the rewritting argument).
```

```
Don't you like the term "mainframe"?

Is an IBM SP a mainframe? It runs a version of UNIX using
RISC6000 processors. But it sure ain't no PC !

Sure there will be more hardware that blurs the line. But there will also
always be the need for ultra high end performance, that CAN'T be
provided by smaller machines. A corollary of Moores law describes
a similar increase in user need for information. So, as hardware and
software gets faster and faster, the demand on it will also increase.

So, to answer your question, I'll call the high end systems & large
data warehouse platforms "Mainframes".

P.S. Have Sperry revamped their clunky ECL yet (since the 80's)?
```

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
- **Date:** 2001-09-07T18:13:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** bit.listserv.ibm-main,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9nb2mb$kmu$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <9naqlp$9tc$1@allday.news.cais.net> <gf7m7.1952$5l6.265043@news1.rdc1.az.home.com>`

```
I compare it to the business of moving stuff.

We have trains, trucks of all sizes, ships, airplanes, bicycles, pipes,
wires, broadcast etc. that can all carry products from the producer to the
consumer.  Sometimes trucks become more economical than trains because they
get closer to the consumer without as much labor intensive loading and
unloading.  Then the trains figure out how to carry truck trailers piggyback
and ships follow suit.

The shipping industry and the airline industry are interesting - they
require massive capital expenditures relative to their expenses and are
subject to price wars because of that - as demand goes up, profit doesn't
necessarily follow.   Successful shipping companies go after long term
contracts and stability.

Mainframe computers require similar commitments, but if you have the volume
to use them efficiently, they are cheaper to run than NT farms.  So IBM is
working to let you piggyback your Unix applications on their mainframes to
save on the labor intensive programming.

Meanwhile, we have mainframes, mini-computers, servers, PCs - if we can
still use such distinctions, at our shop.  And the user doesn't care.  A PC
can be a front end to a Sun, a MVS machine, or another PC - it is the data
that count.  (We also don't care whether our shirt came via train or truck
to our local store).  Costs are calculated behind the scenes.
```

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-09-09T16:29:47+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** bit.listserv.ibm-main,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3b9c0cd7_8@news.newsgroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <9naqlp$9tc$1@allday.news.cais.net> <gf7m7.1952$5l6.265043@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9nb2mb$kmu$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9nb407$l73$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
Could I suggest a reason here?

It has to do with "granularity".

Wherever a "collection" of objects is required to achieve a purpose, "small"
is very often more useful than "big".

Consider the following examples...

1. Building a house from bricks. Selecting a larger brick size will allow
the house to be built with less effort, but it will constrain the dimensions
and details. A balance must be struck. If the artistic integrity is more
important than the time to construct, then smaller bricks are better.

2. A LARGE collection of SMALL objects has more inbuilt redundancy and
consequent durability, than a SMALLer collection of LARGE objects.

Consider ARPANet (from which the Internet largely evolved). It was a
military network designed to sustain heavy damage in the event of Nuclear
attack, without failing completely. Whole nodes could be eliminated but, as
there were thousands of them, command and control traffic could re-route
itself and continue to flow.

3. Consider the mighty constructions built by termites. (720 times their
body size) We would have to build a skyscraper 4320 feet high to equal
this...and it is extremely doubtful that we could do so without machines to
multiply our muscle power...(never mind sticking it all together with shit
and spit...<G>)

4. Look at how the redundancy in bee and ant colonies protects and ensures
the survival of the group.

5. Coming back to less fanciful analogies, say you have a cluster of servers
with 100 servers in it (capable of load levelling and sharing resources),
and you compare this with, say, 5 mainframes also capable of the same
throughput. One mainframe goes down. you lost 20% of your capability. You
would have to have 20 servers simultaneously fail to achieve the same
degradation of service.

This is what I mean by "granularity". The size and number of the particles,
affects the overall effectiveness of the Collection.

You can see similar analogs in many fields and areas. Modular programming
doesn't JUST use small modules because they are more efficient to load and
queue; there is also a certain degree of ease of maintenance when the whole
system doesn't have to be stopped in order to repair one function in it...

Now, I'm sure there will have been huge technical reports written justifying
the decision to go Compaq on performance and price grounds, but I contend
that the granularity concept resides in the subconscious of many system
designers, whether they are aware of it or acknowledge it or not.

Over many years of building things we gain experience, and much of this
experience is buried in concepts that we can't put into words (we just
"know"...<G>). I believe "Granularity" is such a concept.

Pete.
```

```
In article <3b9c0cd7_8@news.newsgroups.com>,
 "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Could I suggest a reason here?
> 
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Yes, but when you factor in the complication of synchronising the data 
across 100 servers, the costs alone mitigate towards a 5 mainframe 
solution. Especially if you want the same response time from 1 
transaction per day as for 1000 transactions per second!

Then there's the backup problem. Just how do you backup the servers data 
such that they are in synchrony. I.e. you can reliably restore the 
entire data set across all servers.

You can't just start from a small simple thing and pile millions of them 
onto each other in the computer world. Well, you can, but you have to 
have the techniques of a mainframe behind you to make the small simple 
thing possible. These techniques degrade when you multiply the number of 
servers. This is because at their heart they require a single lock or 
thread to ensure that every thing happens at the right time.

Mainframe clusters do this by having a whole other mainframe dedicated 
to the management of the data and locks across the cluster.

Super computers don't have this problem, because typically they only 
work on one task at a time. They take the partitioning approach. Each 
element of the super computer performs the same task on a different 
piece of data. A mainframe never knows what transaction will be coming 
next and so cannot make use of the partitioning scheme.
```

```
The Unisys "Mainframes" that I'm talking about are not "server farms."  This
is one monolithic machine that from the outside looks and feels and tastes
like a mainframe.  It just happens to have 32 or 64 Intel processors under
the covers.  It can be configured (dynamically by the way) to look like
several NT boxes, or 1 large MVS box or 1 large MVS with 1 small NT etc.

It's alway interesting how threads migrate.  This started as a discussion of
Internet Explorer.  The point I tried to add was that Microsoft was getting
its fingers into more and more - and that the hardware/software definition
of "Mainframe" was getting muddy.

So the question boils down to (as I put my earmuffs and fireproof suit on )
will the Windows OS lineage ever evolve to the point where, when running on
a super Intel based cluster mainframe, it is considered a mainframe OS?
(*duck and cover!*)
```

```
>  "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > important than the time to construct, then smaller bricks are better.

Nonsense.  Using 'large bricks' does not constrain 'dimensions and
details', the modern way to put up a building is using 'single brick
walls', that are cast on site to the exact dimension and detail that is
required.

> > 2. A LARGE collection of SMALL objects has more inbuilt redundancy and
> > consequent durability, than a SMALLer collection of LARGE objects.

Wrong.  It only gives a superficial appearance of durability.  The
redundancy simply allows a gradual slope of failure rather than large
steps.

The more objects that you have then the more failure points.  If light
bulb have a 'life' of 1000 hours then having 10 10 watt bulbs compared
to 1 100 watt bulb makes no difference because within 2000 hours you
will be in the dark.

> > Consider ARPANet (from which the Internet largely evolved). It was a
> > military network designed to sustain heavy damage in the event of Nuclear
> > attack, without failing completely. Whole nodes could be eliminated but, as
> > there were thousands of them, command and control traffic could re-route
> > itself and continue to flow.

The comparison is flawed.  The alternate to 'many small nodes' was 'many
small nodes'.  Each node was a military base or government site that
still required a computer system regardless of the layout of the
interconnection.

> > 3. Consider the mighty constructions built by termites. (720 times their
> > body size) We would have to build a skyscraper 4320 feet high to equal
> > this...and it is extremely doubtful that we could do so without machines to
> > multiply our muscle power...(never mind sticking it all together with shit
> > and spit...<G>)

This is a flawed comparison.  While we can't build mile high sky
scrapers, we also can't live in termnite colonies.  In fact this
illustrates exactly that no matter how many termite colonies there are
they are all too small for us.

(actually did you see someone is Oz is using old termite colonies to
build an adobe house, he uses the material to cast bricks because it is
all sorted to the right grain size and includes its own cement. But that
is changing lots of small buildings into one large, useful building.)

> > 4. Look at how the redundancy in bee and ant colonies protects and ensures
> > the survival of the group.

In what way do they have the option of using a small number of large
defenders ?  Their choice is not made because because it is betetr, but
because there is no other choice.  Actually some ants have developed
specialist fighters and they have a small number of large ones.

> > 5. Coming back to less fanciful analogies, say you have a cluster of servers
> > with 100 servers in it (capable of load levelling and sharing resources),
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > degradation of service.

The more componets you have the more will be failed at any one time. 
Given a MTBF of 5000 hours then three or four of the hundred servers
will fail each week while with the 5 mainframes there will be a failure
less than once a month.
```

```

On 10-Sep-2001, Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

> > > 2. A LARGE collection of SMALL objects has more inbuilt redundancy and
> > > consequent durability, than a SMALLer collection of LARGE objects.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> will be in the dark.

That depends on your design and maintenance plan.  If you are using an old
fashioned Christmas tree string of lights, you are in the dark as soon as
your worst bulb burns out.  Newer designs work better.  But if you have
redundant lighting, when one light burns out, you can replace it without
losing functionality.  Or if you are Disneyland, you replace bulbs ahead of
time, before they burn out.

The question to ask then is what kind of Christmas tree lights is your data
center made of?


> The more componets you have the more will be failed at any one time.
> Given a MTBF of 5000 hours then three or four of the hundred servers
> will fail each week while with the 5 mainframes there will be a failure
> less than once a month.

Server farms have two big advantages:
1.  Incremental improvements have incremental costs.
2.  The system runs when servers are removed.

But there are still vulnerability points, and certainly cost issues (when
compared to the type of performance possible with a mainframe).
```

```
"Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3B9D987E.8EF279A6@Azonic.co.nz...
> >  "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> >
> > > 1. Building a house from bricks. Selecting a larger brick size will
allow
> > > the house to be built with less effort, but it will constrain the
dimensions
> > > and details. A balance must be struck. If the artistic integrity is
more
> > > important than the time to construct, then smaller bricks are better.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> required.

Are you disagreeing with the statement or the principle behind it, Richard?
Or both...

How could you cast a single brick wall to "exactly the dimension required"
if the dimension required is not a multiple of the brick size? OK, you cut
some bricks, or you change the dimensions. As you may have gathered I am no
expert when it comes to construction of edifices... but I hope the
underlying principle was clear...<G>


> > > 2. A LARGE collection of SMALL objects has more inbuilt redundancy and
> > > consequent durability, than a SMALLer collection of LARGE objects.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Yes, that is an argument. Nevertheless, a gradual slope of failure is
usually preferable to a disastrous big bang which leaves you in the dark. I
agree that granularity needs to be balanced to suit the particular
conditions; I am NOT proposing that small is beautiful in EVERY case. It is
the PRINCIPLE of granularity I was seeking to elucidate.

> The more objects that you have then the more failure points.  If light
> bulb have a 'life' of 1000 hours then having 10 10 watt bulbs compared
> to 1 100 watt bulb makes no difference because within 2000 hours you
> will be in the dark.
>
Nope. You can replace individual bulbs as they fail and the overall total
"luminosity" will only be marginally degraded, for a short period of time.

> > > Consider ARPANet (from which the Internet largely evolved). It was a
> > > military network designed to sustain heavy damage in the event of
Nuclear
> > > attack, without failing completely. Whole nodes could be eliminated
but, as
> > > there were thousands of them, command and control traffic could
re-route
> > > itself and continue to flow.
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

So what is wrong with "many small nodes"? It worked. Again, the "granularity
of the network determined it's overall performance when exposed to trauma.
That is the only point I am trying to make.

> > > 3. Consider the mighty constructions built by termites. (720 times
their
> > > body size) We would have to build a skyscraper 4320 feet high to equal
> > > this...and it is extremely doubtful that we could do so without
machines to
> > > multiply our muscle power...(never mind sticking it all together with
shit
> > > and spit...<G>)
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
No, I didn't see that, but it makes sense. I agree the analogy was fanciful,
but I don't agree it was flawed. It demonstrates the principle of
granularity.

> > > 4. Look at how the redundancy in bee and ant colonies protects and
ensures
> > > the survival of the group.
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
OK, I'll give you that one...<G>. It is a fair point about them having no
choice...

> > > 5. Coming back to less fanciful analogies, say you have a cluster of
servers
> > > with 100 servers in it (capable of load levelling and sharing
resources),
> > > and you compare this with, say, 5 mainframes also capable of the same
> > > throughput. One mainframe goes down. you lost 20% of your capability.
You
> > > would have to have 20 servers simultaneously fail to achieve the same
> > > degradation of service.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> less than once a month.

Yes, but three or four will cause negligible degradation to the overall
service. In fact they will degrade it by 3 or 4 per cent until such time as
they are repaired and back on line. A 20% failure, even every 2 months would
be much more traumatic. This comes back to the point covered above about
"gradual slope of failure".

Bottom line:

The granularity of collections can have a serious effect on the overall
efficacy of the collection.

It is a concept that many designers use intuitively without even being aware
of it.

It is not "right" or "wrong" it just is...

Being aware of it may help someone the next time they address a problem
which involves collections of objects.

Pete.
```

```

On  8-Sep-2001, "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

> Could I suggest a reason here?
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> important than the time to construct, then smaller bricks are better.

Yes - but every choice has its down side.   In this case, the smaller bricks
mean more grout, with more failure points, and more maintenance (nip and
tuck).   Cold air and vermin don't go in through the bricks as much as they
go in through the interfaces.   If my roof is held up by a brick beam vs a
single beam - the single material beam is more likely to survive an
earthquake.

Instead of looking at objects, look at distributed processing.  Each layer
controlled by a different vendor is a different place where things go wrong.
  The cash transactions didn't get posted on the mainframe - where is the
problem?   On the cash machine?  In the TCP/IP packet? On the mainframe?  On
the server?  Lots of points of vulnerability.

Now one way around this can be seen in a similar analogy - Suspension
bridges don't use chains.  Chains are as strong as their weakest link. 
Instead they use cables with lots of redundant wires.  Lots of wires need to
break to cause the cable to fail.   Reinforced brick buildings don't have to
be lost when some bricks get lost.   A challenge is to design an
interdependent computer system so that the failure of one part does not
cause the whole to fail.
```

```
And your conclusion that users don't care, is right on the button, in my
opinion. Neither should they, of course. As long as what is needed is
delivered for the best possible cost, everyone is happy.

It is the people whose living depends on the infrastructure who become
obsessed with a particular aspect of it. This causes a diminution in the
standard of service eventually, and so Market forces are applied to remedy
it.

Then we find technical people looking for a job...

The keyword to survival in today's tech world has to be "adaptability".
Instead of fearing and resisting new technology, embrace it. Make it
better...

It is gratifying to see posters here requesting advice on what books they
should get to learn Java. I really hope as many people as possible will
expand their existing skill sets.

Using your analogy, it is like having a truck licence but realising that a
motor bike and a car licence are also pretty useful...

Thanks for a stimulating post,

Pete.

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> still use such distinctions, at our shop.  And the user doesn't care.  A

> can be a front end to a Sun, a MVS machine, or another PC - it is the data
> that count.  (We also don't care whether our shirt came via train or truck
> to our local store).  Costs are calculated behind the scenes.
```

```
A good response, Rob. I take your point and comment on it below...

Pete.

"rob lec" <roblec@cinci-rr.com> wrote in message
news:3B9D72A4.4CB81CD0@cinci-rr.com...
> >
> > The keyword to survival in today's tech world has to be "adaptability".
> > Instead of fearing and resisting new technology, embrace it. Make it
> > better...
> > It is gratifying to see posters here requesting advice on what books
they
> > should get to learn Java. I really hope as many people as possible will
> > expand their existing skill sets.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> one's hiring"

Your point is good. In a profession where a high "rate of change" is normal,
it is frustrating to spend a lot of time and effort learning stuff well,
only to find that the knowledge attained has a "working life" of maybe 5 or
6 years (if you're lucky...)

There are some exceptions to this and it is no accident that this post is
being made in a COBOL forum...

I sympathise but when I look back over my own career I realise that all of
the effort did me no harm...

Today, I am unlikely to use any of the following (this is by no means an
exhaustive list...:

1. Machine language on NCR 500s (or even SLIP, the assembler for it which
was invented in 1966)

2. IBM 1400 series Autocode.

3. System 360 BAL (.Basic Assembler Language)

4. ICL 1900 PLAN (Program Language Nineteen hundred)

5. NCR 315 CRAM (Card Random Access Memory) programming (a precursor to disk
drives).

6. JEAN - an online BASIC like language for 1900.

7. The concept of DRIVER Beads (again, a bit like CICS for 1900)

8. VSAM

9. CICS

10 IMS DB/DL/1 (or any other Hierarchic Model Database)

11. IDMS, ADABAS or any other Network Model Database...

12. BASIC

(Please note, I am not saying that other people won't use the above, just
that it is unlikely that I will in the future...)

And yet, like many people here, I sweated blood to learn these things and
understand them, only to find that their market value decreased within a few
years to a point where it simply wasn't worth doing it.

So what is the point?

Well, I don't believe I have been harmed in any way by exposure to new
learning experiences and I still get a kick out of mastering something new.

I guess it goes with the territory.

No one forces us to work in an industry where the technology is changing all
the time. We elect to.

It is also true that even stuff we learned and no longer use has some
benefit.

Concepts arise out of it and these can be used as "hooks" to hang new stuff
on.

I don't believe there is any such thing as "useless" information or
knowledge. Sometimes it takes a while for the use of it to become
apparent...<G>

Keep the "yearning for learning" alive and you will not only enjoy your
chosen career more, what is infinitely more important, is you will assist
your personal growth.

Pete.
```

```
Pure MIPs have never really been the basis for deciding if it is a mainframe.  A
good interpretation would be a fair amount of processing power combined with a
hugely large I/O, or in another words, its moves a lot!!!!!!!!!!  of bits.
Supercomputers far outstrip mainframes as far as computational power in
calculation intensive operations, but they don't have the I/O bandwith of a
business mainframe.
```

```
James Johnson <jamesj55@telocity.com> writes:

> Pure MIPs have never really been the basis for deciding if it is a mainframe.  A
> good interpretation would be a fair amount of processing power combined with a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> business mainframe.

actually a lot of "cluster" supercomputers started to significantly
exceed mainframe i/o capacity by the early '90s ... however they
tended to be configured for enormous amounts of disk sequential
transfer as well as enormous amounts of low-latency inter-processor
communication (i.e. in some cases tens of giga-byte channels, not
giga-bit; large numbers 32+8 disk arrays using 10mbyte/sec disks for
individual 320mbyte/sec data transfers, etc).

part of related discussion in this n.g. 18monts ago
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2000b.html#38

also part of the reason that some of the DB2 people claimed that what
my wife and I had done with fiber-channel cluster scale-up and
distributed lock manager was possibly five years ahead of where they
were at.
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/95.html#13 SSA
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/96.html#15 tcp/ip

but notice that nothing prevented them for being configured with lots
of disks for individual transaction processing as opposed to parallel
sequential processing ... in fact, lots of mainframes now have adopted
such installations (i.e. configured with lots of small disks in
various kinds of mirroring &/or raid configurations ... something
picked up from this other market segment).

random refs:
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001i.html#41 Withdrawal Announcement 901-218 - No More 'small machines'
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001i.html#43 Withdrawal Announcement 901-218 - No More 'small machines'
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001i.html#52 misc loosely-coupled, sysplex, cluster, supercomputer, &amp; electronic commerce
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001j.html#16 OT - Internet Explorer V6.0
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001j.html#23 OT - Internet Explorer V6.0
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001j.html#43 Disaster Stories Needed
http://www.garlic.com/~lynn/2001j.html#45 OT - Internet Explorer V6.0
```

```
non-mainframe software does not include this and end the end seems to be
more expensive we when look at a per end user point of view.
```

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-09-14T07:28:37+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** bit.listserv.ibm-main,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BA1A395.1F96E1B2@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <9naqlp$9tc$1@allday.news.cais.net>`

```
Editor wrote:
> What is a "Mainframe"
> How do we define that term?

Much electronic and electromechanical equipment was/is assembled in
racking, cabinets or frames that hold it in place and feed it
resources.  Some frames are subsiduary or peripheral (around the edge)
and some are the most important, largest or main frames. 

The 'main' frame is the cabinet work that held the most important parts,
the central parts, around which the other parts were located in
peripheral frames.  This is how it has been organised in telephone
exchanges, power stations, radio stations, and other electrical systems.

The use of 'mainframe' to mean a particular class of computer is due
entirely to misunderstanding of electrical engineering terminolgy by
computer people.

Any computer system that is held in more than a single cabinet will have
a main frame and subsiduary or peripheral frames.  For any computer that
is in a single cabinet the term does not apply.

> My point was - The line between "Mainframe" and "PC" is bluring very
> quickly.  I don't care if it's MVS/Windows/Unix or some OS yet to be
> invented, the cost/size/technology involved is all mushing together.

Just as long as it isn't a toy OS, such as single-tasking DOS, or BeOS
or Windows of any description. The distinction that makes a system a
'server' is that it will run tasks for many users and can continue
running without having operator intervention.  A Windows PC can never be
called a 'mainframe' in any way.  Powerful though the hardware may be,
Windows is primarily a single-user poke-and-click system, which is fine
as a client machine.
```

```
On 14-Sep-2001, Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

> Any computer system that is held in more than a single cabinet will have
> a main frame and subsiduary or peripheral frames.  For any computer that
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> as a client machine.

If a PC is built using multiple cabinets, then one of those cabinets should
be a mainframe by the definition you gave above.

If a computer is useful to get work done, it is not a toy, and if its OS is
useful to get work done, it is not a toy.   I have seen the definition of
"server" change as Windows changed - seemingly to make sure Windows did not
qualify.  But when I am working with users, I need to use the definitions
they care about.  If their work can be done by a "windows server", then it
is a server.  My arguments about what OS they should use need to be based
upon other criteria such as costs.

My definitions (and yours) don't count for real life decisions.   If we say
"Our server should use Linux because Linux is a real operating system", we
aren't doing our job.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Internet Explorer V6.0

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-09-15T19:23:10+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** bit.listserv.ibm-main,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BA39C8D.853CFAB2@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<9mki96$bit$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <xnkj7.4831$Xv3.806902@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9mlhu0$126n$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <xpUj7.115075$Lw3.7218407@news2.aus1.giganews.com> <o3%j7.16856$Xv3.2681714@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n347g$2css$1@allbad.news.cais.net> <Gg9l7.35570$Xv3.6332535@news1.rdc1.az.home.com> <9n5bom$19o3$1@allgood.news.cais.net> <9naqlp$9tc$1@allday.news.cais.net> <3BA1A395.1F96E1B2@Azonic.co.nz> <4h8o7.308612$NK1.28947439@bin3.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:

> If a PC is built using multiple cabinets, then one of those cabinets should
> be a mainframe by the definition you gave above.

The term 'mainframe computer' only refers to the fact that the computer
is built in various frames or cabinets, one of which is the main one,
usually housing the CPU.  It actually says nothing of the power or
function of the computer, or what brand of components were used, only
how it has been housed.  In fact telephone exchanges and power stations
also have 'main frames' and peripheral frames.

'PC's or 'Personal Computers' are not built into frames or cabinets,
they are in cases.  It is true that components that are often used in
PCs (for example in IBM PC type computers) may also be assembled into
multiple configurations in cabinets or frames for use in servers, but
these are not 'Personal' in any way.

If multiple Pentium processors are rack mounted into a frame and, say,
communications gear is in another frame then this _IS_ a 'mainframe
computer' and is not PC in any way at all.  This is even though those
exact same components may be housed in cases and used as Personal
Computers.

>  I have seen the definition of
> "server" change as Windows changed - seemingly to make sure Windows did not
> qualify. 

That is probably because MS has used tokenism to try to make Windows
qualify under an existing definition.  They have implemented a
minimalist feature and then named it as if it were a realistic
competitor for other systems.  This is to get the 'tick in the box'. 
For example C2 security, POSIX support, 'multi-user', they even stick
'open-source' terms in their marketing when it is no such thing.

Certainly a network of Windows PCs can be used to get work done in an
office environment, but MSN is an example of what happens when Windows
PCs try to replace servers designed for the purpose (BSD in MSN's case).
```

```
Come on, Bill,

when char = "lit-1" also char = "lit-2"

Is wrong and always will be wrong and should be flagged as an error. This is a
particular (and simple) case of misuse. Whether is is syntactic or not is
irrelevant. It's wrong! It's not an element of style; it's WRONG! When a
compiler can issue an error for not placing a period at the end of a pgraph
instead of inserting one, I think it can issue an error in this case where it's
warranted.

Regards, Jack.

>
>The draft standard *does* allow (but not require) compilers to "flag"
…[197 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
Jack, Bill,

A quick 2 cents from me... (I watched this exchange with interest; at first
I couldn't decide who was right because both are valid viewpoints).

The argument that compilers cannot check logic errors is obviously right (as
an old-time compiler writer I have much sympathy for this viewpoint <G>) but
it is a bit dodgy in this case.

The fact is that modern compilers DO check some kinds of error. Where it is
obvious what was intended a "forgiving" compiler will correct it...

case in point:

inspect something
     replacing with spaces

I know of at least one compiler that will change the "with" to "by" and
simply flag a warning.

Now Bill will argue (quite properly) that this is a syntax error.
Nevertheless, it is "corrected".

In Jack's case, how hard can it be to check that an EVALUATE ... ALSO does
not use the SAME operand and flag a warning?

Yes, you can argue, well, where does it end?

But I have great sympathy for the argument that in this case it is OBVIOUS
that what is written is NOT correct (whether it is a "logic" error or a
"syntax" error).

My conclusion:

1. Compiler writers should do everything possible to assist programmers and
the more "forgiving" a compiler is , the more marketable it will be.

2. Point 1 above should not be constrained by an arbitrary rule such as:
"The compiler will ONLY detect syntax errors" or ANY other arbitrary rule.

3. If a compiler writer goes "beyond the rules" it should be because he
chooses to and not because he is REQUIRED to.

On this occasion, I favour Jack's view more than the "letter of the Law".

Pete.
```

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-01T21:15:53-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9ms4q6$atj$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3B8BCD75.8D9B460F@dced.state.ak.us> <f2Zi7.19572$te2.2568523016@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <9mirl9$4ph$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <PFpj7.13869$qh7.1679457324@newssvr17.news.prodigy.com> <3B8E9FB7.15E3CED7@home.com> <9mmdgj$egi$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <pvTj7.76$4z.1018@www.newsranger.com> <9mpc7n$ie4$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net> <Nzbk7.765$4z.1859@www.newsranger.com> <9mrkl1$l42$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3b916fa2_6@goliath.newsgroups.com>`

```
OK,  in the next Standard there are "constants" and conditional
compilations.  What do you do about something like

>> define Constant2 = 9
>>define constant1 = constant2
>>IF SomeOtherConstant = 1
>>define constant2 = Constant2 + 1
>>End-IF

Evaluate Test-Field Also Test-Field
  When Constant1 also Constant2
     Perform sometimes
   When  Constant1 also 9
     Perform othertimes
End-Evaluate

Do you make the compiler issue the message when SomeOtherConstant is *not*
equal to 1?  Do you make it "keep track" of all of this throughout the
compilation? Do you separate the logic for constants (which are SUPPOSED to
be like literals) from literals?

The bottom-line is that in "real world programs" - doing "logic analysis" at
compile time is rarely worthwhile and often impossible (at least without
doing MULTIPLE passes of your source code).

I have NO objections to compilers doing logic analysis WHEN it fits into the
way they handle source code.  I simply object to the (original) idea of
putting this into the STANDARD as something that MUST be done by all
conforming compilers.
```

```
As it is logically, at the very least, "questionable" for a given operand to
simultaneously have two values (Quantum computing is still only
theoretical...<G>), I would argue that the comparison should be flagged as a

and the comparison can therefore succeed, but if I had written this (and I
wouldn't...<G>) I would appreciate the compiler simply getting me to look at
it again.
```

```
WHEN char = var-1 ALSO char = var-2
may look slightly odd, but
WHEN var-1 = char ALSO var-2 = char
looks completely reasonable (but of course they are exactly the same thing).

As Bill says, compilers should be free (but not required) to produce warning
messages if they recognize potential problems in the code logic, but I would
get really fed up with a compiler that flagged the above WHEN conditions.
```

```
While 
EVALUATE FLD-12
    WHEN = "A" and "B"
         GO TO THE-CORNER-YOU-DUMMY

looks absurd,

    WHEN = A and B    (field names)

is completely reasonable.
----------
Incidentally, I am sending a proposal (late!) to J4 to allow the EQUAL
relational operator to be omitted in partial expressions in the
EVALUATE command.
A whole bunch of posters (well, a couple of us!) had assumed that the
"=" was unnecessary, and we were right ... but the proposed syntax
does not currently reflect our correctness! <G>

Stephen J Spiro
Member, J4 COBOL Standards Committee
check it out at 
http://people.ne.mediaone.net/doncobol/

stephenjspiro at hotmail.com

PS: Quick! Join J4! We can still get it right!  But only if there are
enough of us!
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9n2s5g$al9$1@mail.pl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<3B8BCD75.8D9B460F@dced.state.ak.us> <f2Zi7.19572$te2.2568523016@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <9mirl9$4ph$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <PFpj7.13869$qh7.1679457324@newssvr17.news.prodigy.com> <3B8E9FB7.15E3CED7@home.com> <9mmdgj$egi$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <pvTj7.76$4z.1018@www.newsranger.com> <9mpc7n$ie4$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net> <Nzbk7.765$4z.1859@www.newsranger.com> <9mrkl1$l42$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3b916fa2_6@goliath.newsgroups.com> <Yfhk7.417508$T97.50617147@typhoon.nyroc.rr.com> <3b91b98f_3@goliath.newsgroups.com>`

```
My reading of this issue is that the syntax can be generalized to be
    EVALUATE TRUE ALSO TRUE
    WHEN <some completely arbitrary conditional expression>
        ALSO <some other completely arbitrary conditional expression> ...

While it might be possible for some compilers to detect an impossible
relationship between the two boolean expressions in this example, I do not
feel it is the obligation of any to do so.  Precisely because the
conditional expressions are arbitrary, and more particularly there is no
requirement that there be, or not be, any commonality of operands between
them, I would suggest that a compiler that issued a syntax error in this
case would be guilty of precluding something that the standard allows!
Whether the two arbitrary conditional expressions are mutually exclusive is
none of the compiler's business; that's a programmer issue.

I would personally not be in favor of making the conditional expressions in
this case any less arbitrary than they now are,  in either syntax or general
rules.  I wouldn't object if some vendors issued an "oh, by the way ..."
warning, but it's perfectly legal for the user to code a combination that
can never actually exist, just as legal as coding a terminating condition
for a PERFORM that can't ever be "set" from within the PERFORMed logic.

    -Chuck Stevens

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3b91b98f_3@goliath.newsgroups.com...
>
> "William Bub" <fathafluff@hotmail.com> wrote in message
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > The argument that compilers cannot check logic errors is obviously
right
> > (as
> > > an old-time compiler writer I have much sympathy for this viewpoint
<G>)
> > but
> > > it is a bit dodgy in this case.
> > >
> > > The fact is that modern compilers DO check some kinds of error. Where
it
> > is
> > > obvious what was intended a "forgiving" compiler will correct it...
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > I know of at least one compiler that will change the "with" to "by"
and
> > > simply flag a warning.
> > >
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> As it is logically, at the very least, "questionable" for a given operand
to
> simultaneously have two values (Quantum computing is still only
> theoretical...<G>), I would argue that the comparison should be flagged as
a
> warning. I realise that both var-1 and var-2 could contain the same value
> and the comparison can therefore succeed, but if I had written this (and I
> wouldn't...<G>) I would appreciate the compiler simply getting me to look
at
> it again.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EVALUATE problem

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-05T09:42:30-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9n5klc$7r2$1@mail.pl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<3B8BCD75.8D9B460F@dced.state.ak.us> <f2Zi7.19572$te2.2568523016@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <9mirl9$4ph$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <PFpj7.13869$qh7.1679457324@newssvr17.news.prodigy.com> <3B8E9FB7.15E3CED7@home.com> <9mmdgj$egi$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <pvTj7.76$4z.1018@www.newsranger.com> <9mpc7n$ie4$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net> <Nzbk7.765$4z.1859@www.newsranger.com> <9mrkl1$l42$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3b916fa2_6@goliath.newsgroups.com>`

```

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3b916fa2_6@goliath.newsgroups.com...

> The fact is that modern compilers DO check some kinds of error. Where it
is
> obvious what was intended a "forgiving" compiler will correct it...
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> simply flag a warning.

Formally speaking, what the compiler author has done here is provide an
extension to the standard that makes "with" and "by" synonymous in this
context.  "Flag a warning" must note that this is non-standard syntax, not
merely that the compiler has allowed the synonym.

> Now Bill will argue (quite properly) that this is a syntax error.
> Nevertheless, it is "corrected".

I'm not sure it's "corrected" so much as "allowed as a non-standard
alternative".  I think the semantic difference is significant, and as a
non-standard alternative an "extension flag" is required, as is a note in
the compiler documentation.

> In Jack's case, how hard can it be to check that an EVALUATE ... ALSO does
> not use the SAME operand and flag a warning?

Discussed elsewhere.

> But I have great sympathy for the argument that in this case it is OBVIOUS
> that what is written is NOT correct (whether it is a "logic" error or a
> "syntax" error).

Obvious error or not, it's not a *syntax* error because it doesn't violate
any *syntax* rule (or *general* rule, for that matter).

< My conclusion:
>
> 1. Compiler writers should do everything possible to assist programmers
and
> the more "forgiving" a compiler is , the more marketable it will be.

One could argue that a "forgiving" compiler "forgives" the programmer for
making logic errors that aren't prohibited by the syntax rules!   I agree,
but we might differ as to what "forgiving" means!

> 2. Point 1 above should not be constrained by an arbitrary rule such as:
> "The compiler will ONLY detect syntax errors" or ANY other arbitrary rule.

On the other hand, the compiler should not unconditionally prevent
compilation of a *syntactically*-correct program.  For example, most
compilers don't "trace" the logic flow of a user program sufficiently to
ensure that an OPEN statement is actually executed on a file before a READ
or WRITE is executed against it; so far as I know, most compilers don't even
check to see whether an OPEN statement exists in the program for the file,
for that matter.  Attempting to do a READ or WRITE on an unopened file is, I
think, a logic error.  I don't mind warnings on *logic* errors like this,
but I don't think they should be treated as *syntax* errors.  Nor do I think
the standard should mandate a new class, "logic errors".

> 3. If a compiler writer goes "beyond the rules" it should be because he
> chooses to and not because he is REQUIRED to.

I agree; in this case, though, the idea seems to be that the rules should be
modified so that the detection of such logic errors is no longer "beyond the
rules", and I'm opposed to that.   I think the *syntax* rules are fine just
as they are in this respect.

> On this occasion, I favour Jack's view more than the "letter of the Law".

Again, I don't have any objection to a warning.  I don't object to a
compiler writer making it *possible* for the user to receive a syntax error
instead of a warning for this (or any other) logic error, so long as that is
not the default behavior.  I do object to a conformant compiler
unconditionally issuing a *syntax* error (and thereby preventing
compilation) for this case, because I believe that the usage is
*syntactically* correct.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
I am amazed at the responses generated by this post.

I can't figure whether people simple don't read it properly or are blinkered
by their own viewpoint.

There is really no need for screeds on this.

No-one is saying that an extension to the Standard should be made to allow
logic checking. That would be patently ridiculous.

All I am saying (and it is a very reasonable position (like most of those I
adopt <G>)) is that there are occasions where a forgiving compiler can
detect and correct certain types of coding "error". When/if it does this
would be at the discretion of the compiler writer, it would not be flagged
as a fatal error (purely informational), and the compiler would NOT be
considered non-compliant with any existing or proposed standard because it
did so.

How difficult is that?

The evaluate example is a very good case in point. I would like it if my
compiler flagged the fact that I was evaluating two constants (or variables,
for that matter) against the SAME field (which can only ever have ONE value)
at the SAME time.

The reason I would like it flagged is because I may have inadvertently coded
some similar field names and got it wrong...

It has nothing to do with Standards and it never did have.

Further comments below. (And I won't be responding further on this after
that... It isn't important enough to warrant all that's been written about
it.)

Pete.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<4145699b.0109092251.171bc6c7@posting.google.com>`

```
This thread got me to thinking (what a STRANGE feeling!), so I
submitted the following paper to J4 just now.  If anyone has comments,
send them to Don Schricker, or send them to me and I will forward
themn to J4.
Note to Pete Dashwood: this is why we don't 'timeline' topics.  Some
things are subtle enough that it takes a long time to catch them.

Stephen J Spiro
Member, J4 COBOL Standards Committee
check it out at 
http://people.ne.mediaone.net/doncobol/

stephenjspiro at hotmail.com
_________________________________________________________

Subject:	"Partial expressions" in EVALUATE

Author:	Stephen J Spiro
	Wizard Systems


References:

1. Base Document:  FCD

PROPOSED REVISION:

Make "=" optional when it is the leftmost operator in a partial
expression.
 
JUSTIFICATION:

In a discussion in comp.lang.cobol of the new features in EVALUATE,
several otherwise knowledgeable participants (blush!) made the mistake
of including examples which omitted the equal sign in partial
expressions. For instance,
		EVALUATE	something	ALSO	something-else
		    WHEN     	A or B		ALSO	Field-C and Field-D
				Do....

Mr Klein and others pointed out that, since the rule is
"5) A selection object is a partial-expression if the leftmost portion
of the selection object is a relational operator,
a class condition without the identifier, a sign condition without the
identifier, or a sign condition without the
arithmetic expression.",

the current syntax would require
		EVALUATE	something	ALSO	something-else
		    WHEN    	= A or B	ALSO	= Field-C and Field-D
				Do....

The omission of partial expressions for EVALUATE in the last Standard
has been the cause of much foul language in programming organizations,
and this correction of the omission was solidly in place before I
joined the committee.

Requiring the physical expression of the EQUAL operator will continue
the foul language; there is no need for it, and it "reads funny".

There has never been a requirement for the equal sign in a case such
as
		EVALUATE	something	ALSO	something-else
		        WHEN	E		ALSO	Field-F
				Do....
But, meta-syntactically, "E" is the same as "A or B", and "Field-F" is
the same as "Field-C and Field-D".

Let's fix it now, before the complaints flood in as the new standard
is implemented.

COMPATIBILITY:

This feature is compatible with ANSI X3.23-1985 as amended by ANSI
X3.23a-1989 and ANSI X3.23b-1993.

"Partial condition" is not in the index. I believe the term is not
used anywhere in the standard except under the EVALUATE instruction.

PROPOSED CHANGES TO BASE DOCUMENT:

1. Page 449, 14.10.12.2, Add at the end of General Rule 5.  "If the
leftmost operator in a partial expression represents equality, that
operator may be implied; that is, it need not be coded explicitly."


RESOLVED ISSUES: 
This will handle the equal sign, EQUALS, IS EQUAL TO, and all the word
permutations.  Note that if the operator is negated, the "NOT EQUAL,
etc" would still be required, because that operator is INequality.


OPEN ISSUES:	None
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<3B8BCD75.8D9B460F@dced.state.ak.us> <f2Zi7.19572$te2.2568523016@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <9mirl9$4ph$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <PFpj7.13869$qh7.1679457324@newssvr17.news.prodigy.com> <3B8E9FB7.15E3CED7@home.com> <9mmdgj$egi$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <pvTj7.76$4z.1018@www.newsranger.com> <9mpc7n$ie4$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net> <Nzbk7.765$4z.1859@www.newsranger.com>`

```
On Sat, 01 Sep 2001 20:30:37 GMT, John H Sleight Jr
<nospam@newsranger.com> wrote:

>Come on, Bill,
>
>when char = "lit-1" also char = "lit-2"
>
>Is wrong and always will be wrong and should be flagged as an error.

Sorry to disagree, but this is not wrong!  It is plainly a construct
that is always false, but it _is_ syntactically correct.

As taken from my example to Jimmy

EVALUATE TRUE ALSO TRUE
   WHEN The-char = 'A' ALSO The-char = 'B'
      DISPLAY 'Obviously, this can never be true'
   WHEN Gender='male' ALSO Gender='female'
      DISPLAY 'Should the compiler flag this as error'
      DISPLAY 'Or should he accept this statement?'
   WHEN Gender-is-male ALSO Gender-is-female
      DISPLAY 'Should the compiler flag this as error'
      DISPLAY 'Or should he accept this statement?'
   WHEN AGE > 2000 ALSO (Race-is-humand AND 'GENDER = 'male')
      DISPLAY 'And what about this?  
   WHEN Job = 'US-President' ALSO His-Name = 'Al Gore'
      DISPLAY 'That happens if you don''t trust computers'
      DISPLAY 'when they do make the decisions'
END-EVALUATE


I believe that all of the above are syntactically correct, and there
is no reason the compiler should complain about anything

If the compiler were to flag all logic/semantic errors - wow, would
programming be ever so easy...
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EVALUATE problem

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9n6mda$ppd$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3B8BCD75.8D9B460F@dced.state.ak.us> <f2Zi7.19572$te2.2568523016@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <9mirl9$4ph$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <PFpj7.13869$qh7.1679457324@newssvr17.news.prodigy.com> <3B8E9FB7.15E3CED7@home.com> <9mmdgj$egi$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <pvTj7.76$4z.1018@www.newsranger.com>`

```
Pete (and the rest of the group),

At the bottom of this note is the first note in the thread that talked about
a message for the "logic error".

I don't think that it was unreasonable for me (or others) to "assume" that
the comment,

 "A warning msg, at minimum, is called for. How about it, committee guys?"

seemed to be asking for the Standard to *require* this "logic check" - and
to issue (potentially MORE than a warning message).

My sense is that there is now consensus (not unanimity) that the Standard
(if you believe in a Standard at all - which is an entirely separate issue)
should *require* error messages for syntax errors and should ALLOW all
source code that is syntactically correct.

In addition, any "warning" (informational, other?) messages that the
compiler can produce to indicate potential logic errors is "nice to have".
(In fact, they may be a marketing advantage to those compiler vendors that
produce them.)  However, the compiler *must* not stop the programmer from
writing conforming source code that THEY want - even if the compiler writer
thinks that what is coded is "logically impossible".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EVALUATE problem

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** Charles <nospam@newsranger.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-06T16:00:09+00:00

- **Message-ID:** `<d4Nl7.6643$4z.26597@www.newsranger.com>`
- **References:** `<3B8BCD75.8D9B460F@dced.state.ak.us> <f2Zi7.19572$te2.2568523016@newssvr15.news.prodigy.com> <9mirl9$4ph$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <PFpj7.13869$qh7.1679457324@newssvr17.news.prodigy.com> <3B8E9FB7.15E3CED7@home.com> <9mmdgj$egi$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <pvTj7.76$4z.1018@www.newsranger.com> <9n6mda$ppd$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
On the AS400 there is a Source Edit Utility (SEU) that does syntax checking
while the programmer is writing the code.  The problem is that it can be a real
pain because it can halt your progress if you can't figure out right away how to
fix the problem.  It stops you from doing work sometimes while you are writing
or trying to save your code.  I would prefer a syntax checker that gives little
hints in popup boxes or something like that, but if I am typing I want to be
able to keep going like a bat out of hell.

I have seen some PC interactive compilers that mark your code or give you an
error list that allows you to jump to the line with the error.  I thought that
was nice.  Most important is the ability to turn the bells and whistles off.
The Microsoft Visual C++ compilers have things like auto completion for complex
commands and functions, that use pop-up boxes for PC compilers.

A good option might be a just a source editor that lets you write your code on a
PC with all the bells and whistles that is designed for 80 column source and
then allows you to upload it to the mainframe, that way if the network goes down
you can keep working.  It would be nice if it could automatically update your
code by checking the mainframe copy for changes and then just update your source
on the mainframe when you are done.  I still kind of like the older editor that
came with the Micro Focus Personal Cobol compiler.

We use a Decision Analyzer product from DTI that uses EHLLAPI to make reports
and downloads and you build the reports from the PC and it just sends data back
and forth from the PC to the Mainframe.  Basically EHLLAPI products piggyback
their code through a mainframe emulator like IBM Personal Communications and use
functions to send data and return codes back and forth. It was written in
Assembly on the mainframe and the PC client (I Think) was written in VB. A
source editor could utilize that kind of technology and allow the use of a PC
interface that uses a mouse and windows style API's like VB, C++, or maybe Java.


In article <9n6mda$ppd$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>, William M. Klein says...
>
>Pete (and the rest of the group),
…[47 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:9n6mda$ppd$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net...

> My sense is that there is now consensus ... that the Standard ... should
*require* error messages for syntax errors ...

Requiring an error message for anything outside the standard pretty much
precludes implementor extensions because most of them would be syntax errors
in strict standard COBOL.  This is rather stricter than I'd be comfortable
with.  The FCD allows extensions, so long as they're flagged by the compiler
and documented as such; I'm OK with that.  If a vendor decides to allow
"WITH" instead of "BY" in INSPECT, for example, I'd expect this flagged as
an extension in the documentation and by the compiler at compile-time; I
wouldn't insist that it always be a syntax error.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>

```
The FCD also allows implementors to define SYNTAX (as extensions).
Therefore, I stand by my statement that the Standard should require error
messages for syntax error.  However, to clarify, syntax errors are those
pieces of syntax that violate the syntax as defined by that implementor
which includes all of the Standard - and anything they have *added*.

By added, I include things such as:

A) "This restriction is removed"

B) Format 1 may also be used for xyz type items

C) The word "ABC" may be used instead of "XYZ"

D) there is a new XYZ statement or phrase
```

```

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com> wrote in message
news:3B8FFFF6.53754C10@home.com...

> What's the current N. American usage ? When I came from
> UK sofa, settee, became chesterfield. Now it appears they are referred to
as
> sofas.

The correct usage is "chaise longue", "long chair".  American English has it
pronounced pretty much like "Shay's long".  Personally, I tend to something
closer to "shehz long".   "Chaise lounge" (promounced as "shay's lounge" is
listed in the Ninth Collegiate as an alternative by folk etymology.   Both
forms are most often used in the US to refer to patio furniture.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
I grew up calling it a couch.  But when I did hear therm, it was pronounced
"chase lounge".  French was a language I gave up before learning to
pronounce anything - most languages have MORE logical pronunciation than
English, not less - so I have no idea what the French pronunciation should
be.

Just did a quick check at yourdictionary.com and it has:
Main Entry: chaise longue
                                           Pronunciation: 'shAz-'lo[ng]
                                           Function: noun
                                           Etymology: French, literally,
       long chair
                                           Date: 1800
                                           Inflected Form(s): plural chaise
       longues also chaises longues
                                           /'shAz-'lo[ng](z)/
                                           : a long reclining chair

I suppose this must be the French pronunciation, but I would need to hear it
to know how it goes.  That [ng] makes me think it might have that nasal
quality of French speach.

 Meanwhile on both sides of the Atlantic, there are lots of cases of
 contenental words having changed pronunciations when imported.  And
 occasionally Americans are closer to the original than the Brits.
```

```

Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote in message
news:9n312g$hv8$1@peabody.colorado.edu...

> I suppose this must be the French pronunciation, but I would need to hear
it
> to know how it goes.  That [ng] makes me think it might have that nasal
> quality of French speach.

The Ninth Collegiate has the second word of "chaise longue" pronounced
exactly the same as the English word "long".  My mother, who spent twelve
years as a student at a French-speaking school run by Parisian nuns in New
York City, pronounced it indistinguishably from "long".   I haven't yet
heard a native speaker of French pronounce it in a way that my ear can
distinguish the difference, and I was trained to detect and record such
differences as the IPA provides for at one point in my studies.

To whatever degree the "o" is nasalized in "longue", the "ng" must be
explicitly pronounced as a separate consonant group because of the trailing
vowels, according to the rules of French.

>  Meanwhile on both sides of the Atlantic, there are lots of cases of
>  contenental words having changed pronunciations when imported.  And
>  occasionally Americans are closer to the original than the Brits.

Sometimes; sometimes it's the other way 'round, and sometimes it varies all
over the place depending on which part of the country you're from.  I don't
think that's exclusive to continental words or to the US; it's simply an
issue of dialects.  Since I grew up in South Central Texas, I tend to cringe
when I hear "row-DAY-oh".  In the dialect of that area, the preferred
pronunciation is "ROW-dee-oh".   Same for "PEE-can" or "PEE-kahn" or
"puh-CAN" for pecan (of Algonquin origin, I gather); where I come from, it's
pronounced "puh-KAHN".

       -Chuck Stevens
```

```
I recommend to you both "Made in America" by Bill Bryson, which is a sort of
irreverent potted social history of the US, with language as a starting
point.

He points out that in Shakesperian English, "Vermin" was pronounced
"Varmint", "soldier" was "sojer", "touchy" was "Tetchy".

So, by his estimation, you should go no further for a model of Shakespearian
English than Yosemite Sam.
```

```
On Tue, 4 Sep 2001 11:17:44 -0700, "Chuck Stevens"
<charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>
>Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote in message
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>differences as the IPA provides for at one point in my studies.
<snipped>

As a fluent speaker of French and English, I can assure you that there
is a significant difference between the pronunciation of "long" and
"longue", be that in Quï¿½bï¿½cois, in Parisian, or in southern French.

Since you appear to be interested, to approximate the difference,
 in pronouncing "long", 
the tongue is pushed fully against the upper incisors, and the lips
form a circle,
whereas in pronouncing  "longue",
the tongue is pushed against the palate, just above the upper
incisors, and the lips are pursed.

BTW - I didn't say it was easy ...  

Robaire
```
