###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: filler

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-09-25T07:51:31+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BB02973.98118226@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<r1kr7.66661$6x5.14777398@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <93nsqtgqlrr8bbadc4j7fea7rbi00urcfa@4ax.com> <3BAF384D.AC1C9D56@Azonic.co.nz>`

```
JerryP wrote:
> >
> > 01  FOO-REPORT-LINE.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >    03 FOO-COMPANY-BALANCE PIC -(9)9.99.

> Moving space to a group item containing a numeric definition 

It is not a 'numeric' field, it is an 'edited numeric' field.  The
remainder of your argument is invalid as it is based on this lack of
observation.

> 'would be more useful' in rare cases. 
> While this is one of these rare cases (because the
> programmer immediately move a number to the defined numeric field), moving
> spaces to a group item is, in general, a practice almost guaranteed to
> result in problems.

Complete nonsense.  This is not a 'rare case' at all.  Nor does it have
anything to do with 'immediately moving a number to the field' (because
it is not numeric).  One can move spaces to this group field and then
use it for its intended purpose (writing to printer) without any other
moves, with no danger at all.

In fact it is common practice that report data lines redefine each
other, either explicitly in working-storage or implicitly in file
section, and the only way to guarantee they do not contain junk is to
space fill the longest (with no possibility of 'problems').

This thread is on 'FILLER'.  Given the _report_line_ definition the
_only_ way to ensure that the inter-field gaps are space filled is to
use a group move of spaces.  The INITIALISE simply does not work as it
skips FILLER items.  Of course the FILLERs could be given names to make
the INITIALISE do the required work, but then it becomes a negative
example on the use of FILLER.
```

#### ↳ Re: filler

- **From:** Robaire <robaireRemoveThis@sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T09:41:01-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d3duqto5itjlbbbk1f5551fejn6nhk69t9@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<r1kr7.66661$6x5.14777398@afrodite.telenet-ops.be>`

```
On Sun, 23 Sep 2001 11:51:51 GMT, "Luc" <luc.claerebout@pandora.be>
wrote:

>Can anyone explain to me the significance of the filler instruction,  with
>an example would be nice.
>Thanks in advance.

In teaching COBOL, I used the following explanation of FILLER, 
which appeared to clarify its use for most:
Imagine that your going to process a file of personnel records that
contain, for each employee, values for the following fields
Employee Number, 
Name, 
Yearly Salary,  
Address, City, 
Postal Code, 
Telephone Number, , 

Admittedly, the example is contrived, but please bear with me...

Your program is going to print mailing labels.
Are there fields in the file that you do not need in order to print
mailing labels ?
Yes - Salaray is one of them.

In other words, only give access to fields that are required;
Give access on a _NEED_TO_KNOW_ basis.

In COBOL, this might be coded as such:

01 EmployeeRecord.
      05  Name                                PIC X(20).
      05  FILLER                             PIC X(8).
      05  Address                           PIC X(20).
      05  City                                     PIC X(20).
      05  Postal Code                    PIC X(6)
      05  Telephone Number      PIC X(10).

Notice that where the salary would be expected to appear, we used the
name FILLER. This means that we can't reference to characters that
represent the salary in our program. We don't need to know the salary,
so we can't access it. The digits are in there, but we can't extract
them - nor do we need to.

By the way, we also don't need the telephone numbre to address labels.
So we should have coded 

      05  FILLER                              PIC X(10).

instead of 
      05  Telephone Number      PIC X(10).

Robaire
```

#### ↳ Re: filler

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T16:59:24+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BAF67BC.6365E90A@home.com>`
- **References:** `<r1kr7.66661$6x5.14777398@afrodite.telenet-ops.be>`

```


Luc wrote:

> Can anyone explain to me the significance of the filler instruction,  with
> an example would be nice.
> Thanks in advance.

Luc,

The others have given you all sorts of examples using FILLERS, but here's one
that came up in a recent thread where somebody wanted to add an ISBN to an
EXISTING record.

01 MyBookRecord.
  05 BookPrimeKey    pic x(05).
  05 BookName          pic x(40).
  05 BookAuthor        pic x(20).
  05 Book etc.........
  05 Filler                    pic x(30).

Assume you didn't have the FILLER and you wanted to add an ISBN (pic x(15)) or
maybe the Book Price (pic 9(03)v9(02)) - you are stumped - you have to create
a new file record, read in the old file format and output the new file format
with these two additional fields - and INITIALIZE BOTH !.

If your record ALREADY contains the FILLER then your new record format for the
EXISTING file moves from :-

  05 Book etc.........
  05 Filler                    pic x(30).

to :-

  05 Book etc.........
  05 BookISBN                    pic x(15).
  05 BookPrice                     pic 9(03)v9(02).
  05 Filler                              pic x(10).

You should still update the new format, READ NEXT record, initialize BookISBN
and BookPrice to spaces and zeroes respectively, then REWRITE record.

I'll be perfectly honest with you -  I don't use FILLERs in this way very
often, but I think you will appreciate in a large installation with a long
history of processing, (I believe it was Skippy who just said he found an
installation still using programs written in COBOL 68 !), and where the record
format has grown to accommodate new requirements, then it becomes a godsend to
have this type of 'extra' at the end of the record to cover what may be coming
up in the future - that is, until the application gets completely revamped.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: filler

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T17:13:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9onphi$jbq$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<r1kr7.66661$6x5.14777398@afrodite.telenet-ops.be> <3BAF67BC.6365E90A@home.com>`

```
Another example of filler:


05  MY-SEQUENCE.
     10  My-SEQUENCE-NUMERIC   PIC X(05).
     10  FILLER                                PIC X.

...

IF MY-SEQUENCE-NUMERIC IS NUMERIC
        MOVE MY-SEQUENCE    TO VALID-SOLUTION
ELSE
        PERFORM EDIT-ROUTINE.

Reference modification works, but isn't as clear.
```

#### ↳ Re: COMPUTE verb

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T18:39:53+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BAF7F48.5B62BAF8@home.com>`
- **References:** `<bHAr7.1340$4Y1.899975@news1.news.adelphia.net>`

```


Ethan M Giordano wrote:

> Can someone point me to a good tutorial or reference source on the web, or
> just quickly explain how to do rounding with the COMPUTE verb. I can't find
> the info anywhere.
>
> ~Ethan

Check out 'All Things COBOL' for other links. See if you can get university
sites. Unfortunately I don't have the link but check U. of Limerick, Ireland -
they have some good demo source on-line

A very broad answer to your question :-

01 Amount1    pic 9(03)v9(02) value 12.35.
01 Amount2    pic 9(03)v9(02) value 1.23.
01 Amount3    pic 9(03)v9(02).

compute Amount3 ROUNDED = Amount1 + Amount2

BEWARE - most of the time COMPUTE works, but when you start fiddling with
fields of different sizes, with varying numbers for decimal content - you don't
always the get the answer you expected. Sometimes this necessitates splitting
the COMPUTE into several individual COMPUTEs before arriving at your final
answer.
This is not an every day problem with things like dollars and cents, but can
sure be troublesome when trying to do something like the following :-

      *-------------------------------------------------------------
      *
      *   Compute Critical  =                ( P * R )
      *                                   -----------------------------
      *                                      ( ( S * JE ) - ( P * 0.6 ) )
      *
      *-------------------------------------------------------------

You are unlikely to come up against the above at the learning stage <G>

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

*>----------------rounded1.cbl ---------------------------------
01 Amount1              pic 9(03)v9(03) value 12.356.

compute Amount3 rounded = Amount1 + Amount2.  *> = 013.59

## Thread: ODBC and MS Excel — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### ODBC and MS Excel

- **From:** thepla@attglobal.net (Stephen Plotnick)
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T10:42:23-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<280fe6c7.0109240942.4260f080@posting.google.com>`

```
I would like to use SQL statements to create a MS Excel spreadsheet on
the fly. I do not need to do any updates or deletes. I just need to be
able to create a spreadsheet with data that I now write to a file and
later on import into Excel manually. I am using CA-Realia and have
been very successful reading/writing/updating/deleting, etc. with MS
Access via SQL statements.
```

#### ↳ Re: ODBC and MS Excel

- **From:** lsunley@mb.sympatico.ca
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T18:52:01+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ZpzG4UNLyRNq-pn2-JpFf1ePOlGkP@thishost>`
- **References:** `<280fe6c7.0109240942.4260f080@posting.google.com>`

```
On Mon, 24 Sep 2001 17:42:23, thepla@attglobal.net (Stephen Plotnick) 
wrote:

> I would like to use SQL statements to create a MS Excel spreadsheet on
> the fly. I do not need to do any updates or deletes. I just need to be
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Access via SQL statements.

SQL does not create Excel spreadsheets.

There are OLE functions (or whatever it is called now) that allow you 
to manipulate an Excel spreadsheet from within a program.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: ODBC and MS Excel

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net>
- **Date:** 2001-09-24T20:20:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<JAMr7.757$SV1.276846@paloalto-snr1.gtei.net>`
- **References:** `<280fe6c7.0109240942.4260f080@posting.google.com> <ZpzG4UNLyRNq-pn2-JpFf1ePOlGkP@thishost>`

```
The ODBC SQL statement "CREATE TABLE" will create a new "sheet" in Excel;
UPDATE and SELECT work to retrieve rows and columns from existing sheets
(tables). (SELECT columnname FROM sheetname....UPDATE sheetname SET ....)

I have not figured out how to "create" the spreadsheet file itself, but if
you have a "*.xls"  file, you can do these things without resorting to OLE.

(Actually, I haven't been able to figure out how to set up an ODBC DSN or
ODBC file DSN for Excel without the *.xls file already existing).
```

#### ↳ Re: ACU Cobol function keys

- **From:** mattr@mentis.com.au (Matt)
- **Date:** 2001-09-25T20:53:10-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<384ccc85.0109251953.4f935927@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3bafea3d$1@news.actrix.gen.nz>`

```
"ocsmith" <ocsmith@actrix.co.nz> wrote in message news:<3bafea3d$1@news.actrix.gen.nz>...
> We are currently converting our hospital system from Micofocus Dos based
> Cobol to ACU Windows. Is there any easy way to detect F11 F12 and other keys
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> OC

Look up Appendix M (M.2.2) in the Appendices Manual (book 4).  Also
check out the KEYSTROKE config variable.

You will be adding entries to the config file like so :

keystroke exception=11 U1   

(this will return an exception value of 11 for function key f11)

Good luck.

Matt Robinson
Mentis Technologies
```

#### ↳ Re: Cobol 2000

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-25T17:07:37-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<3bb04cfd$1@netnews.web.de>`

```
Besides what is in the FAQ (that Thane pointed to),  a few comments in
response.

A) There is no such thing as a "2000" COBOL Standard.  There *is* a draft
Standard that the current best estimates indicate will be approved at the
end of 2002.

B) A good "overview" (not quite current - but still useful) describing the
OO features can be found (in PDF format) at:
  http://www.adtools.com/info/whitepaper/oocoby2k.pdf

C) The current FULL draft Standard can be found from the web page at:
 www.ncits.org/tc_home/j4.htm
If you download that LARGE document, then look at the two appendices
  substantive changes - potential effecting
  substantive changes - not effecting
that will give you a complete list of the major changes in the draft
Standard.

D) There are a NUMBER of implementations that already support OO COBOL - as
an extension to the existing ANSI/ISO Standards.  Unfortunately, none of
them exactly match the current draft Standard - nor do any of them match the
others.

E) I notice that your email ID ends with ".de" - which as I recall is a
German address.  If so, you might want to contact the German COBOL group.  I
don't have an exact address to get to the whole group, but if you contact
Artur Reimann at:
  reimann <at> attglobal.net
I think he would be able to help you with any questions/issues that you
might have from a specific "German" perspective.
```

```
The following is a long overdue response to the question asked on the
alternative means of qualification I would like to see.

FILE-NAME.RECORD-NAME.GROUP-NAME.FIELD-NAME (subscript) would be the
same as FIELD-NAME OF GROUP-NAME OF RECORD-NAME OF FILE-NAME (subscript)
and FILE-NAME.RECORD-NAME.GROUP-NAME.FIELD-NAME (position : length)
would be
the same as FIELD-NAME OF GROUP-NAME OF RECORD-NAME OF FILE-NAME
(position : length).  In both cases the fully qualified name would be
modified. This proposal mirrors the way qualification is done in most
other languages.  It would not work for fully numeric paragraph or
section names but I would sincerely hope that these are virtually
non-existent.

completeness it should be accepted.  In one sense it is cosmetic but I
have had more than one person tell me that code with qualified names is
hard to read and I find it more difficult.  The current method of
qualification also takes 3 characters per level more than my proposal. 
If you want to stay on one line for a statement as much as possible, use
meaningful names and use a screen font that doesn't require a magnifying
glass (or keep fixed format), this is not a trivial consideration.

```
I like MOVE CORRESPONDING only for specialized applications, such as a
one-time unload reload.

In real life, I prefer having unique prefixes or suffixes when the file
formats are the same.  With an IDD usually this is easy, with the old style
copy members, this is clumsy and unclear.

I don't like having the same element names in completely different records.
Make it easy to determine where the element is.

Of  course, the syntax  MYFILE.MYELEMENT is just about as easy as
MF-MYELEMENT, and even clearer.  A shop can have this standard without
accepting MOVE CORRESPONDING in production jobs.
```

In answer to the question below assume someone was idiotic enough to do
the following:  All sections have names that are 1000 thru 100000 in
increments of 1000 and paragraphs within each section with names in the
range 010 through 990.  How does the compiler distinguish between the
qualified name 2000.020 and the literal 2000.020.  I suspect that it is
possible for the compiler to make the distinction because paragraph
names are alway the target of a PERFORM or a GO TO and literals never
are.  This discussion of course ignores the fact that any attempt to
introduce programs with all numeric paragraph names shows complete lack
of sense on the part of the person doing so.

Clark,

I don't think this is a problem.  When qualifying via a paragraph name, you are
using a Perform, a GO TO or some other construct that references a label in the
procedure division.  Did you know that with Ansi 85 COBOL you can have a
paragraph name that is the SAME as a data name?

I have seen one compiler choke on this and behave strangely -- but it compiled
and most allow it just fine.

The upshot is, that I don't yet think this would cause a problem for a compiler
in figuring out when it is a literal vs when it is a qualified data name.  No
matter how stupid it is to do!

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Alternative qualification was Re: MOVE CORRESPONDING - Was Re: R:

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-27T21:22:56-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9p0muc$e8a$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3b53a7e9$1@newsgate.hknet.com> <9j0lmv$7hv$1@fe2.cs.interbusiness.it> <m4r7ltgmu1rcd0tndapols0pqut2dudavj@4ax.com> <2s_47.22490$Kf3.292704@www.newsranger.com> <3B5482F7.17FC53AF@home.com> <3b555404_1@news2.newsgroups.com> <9j4cd7$cvv$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <3b561f38_2@news2.newsgroups.com> <3B5EC2A9.279DC7A6@cusys.edu> <3B6897F4.DE32C2FB@istar.ca> <FSma7.15792$ar1.57837@www.newsranger.com> <3BB119A6.2BDB3A18@istar.ca> <HVls7.5894$ev2.9895@www.newsranger.com> <3BB284E1.5E5B5424@istar.ca> <5wKs7.7391$ev2.12578@www.newsranger.com>`

```
Thane - as I often say "Huh???"

Page IV-6 and  IV-7 of the '85 Standard is QUITE clear that,

"4.2.2.1.1 User-Defined Words
    ...
Within a given source program, but excluding any contained program, the
user-defined words are grouped into the following disjoint sets:
      ...
 4. condition-names, data-names, and record-names
     ...
  9. paragraph-names
    ...
All user-defined words, except segment-numbers and level-numbers, can belong
to one and only one of these disjoint sets."

   ***
If you have a compiler that allows paragraph names to be the same as a
data-name in the same program, then that compiler had BETTER flag it when
ANSI (or FIPS) flagging is requested.

NOTE: There is a change in the '85 Standard (compared to earlier ones) that
allows for non-unique and impossible to be made unique by qualification
data-names OR procedure-names - however, this is only true when they are not
referenced and this does NOT mean that you can have the same "never used"
procedure-name and data-name.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Alternative qualification was Re: MOVE CORRESPONDING - Was Re: R:

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-09-28T15:25:38+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bb3ee65_8@news.newsgroups.com>`
- **References:** `<3b53a7e9$1@newsgate.hknet.com> <9j0lmv$7hv$1@fe2.cs.interbusiness.it> <m4r7ltgmu1rcd0tndapols0pqut2dudavj@4ax.com> <2s_47.22490$Kf3.292704@www.newsranger.com> <3B5482F7.17FC53AF@home.com> <3b555404_1@news2.newsgroups.com> <9j4cd7$cvv$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <3b561f38_2@news2.newsgroups.com> <3B5EC2A9.279DC7A6@cusys.edu> <3B6897F4.DE32C2FB@istar.ca> <FSma7.15792$ar1.57837@www.newsranger.com> <3BB119A6.2BDB3A18@istar.ca> <HVls7.5894$ev2.9895@www.newsranger.com> <3BB284E1.5E5B5424@istar.ca> <5wKs7.7391$ev2.12578@www.newsranger.com> <9p0muc$e8a$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
When I learned COBOL there was a "rule" which said that ALL Names MUST begin
with an Alphabetic Character (and not exceed 30 characters in length).

This would preclude the possibility of having purely numeric procedure
names.

Has the "Standards Authority" in their infinite wisdom seen fit to revoke
this simple and easily remembered rule, sometime in the last 30 years, Bill?

Of course, being a dinosaur, I still code according to this rule, and you
know what? It has never caused me a problem...<G>

I have watched this thread with extreme amusement.

There is no reason why "dot" qualification CANNOT be part of COBOL as it is
in C and Java, but there is no reason why it SHOULD be either...

The rules regarding indexing and reference modification for such fields are
already well established, but we should change them to comply with the "way
that all the other languages do it"?

It isn't worth the time being wasted on it, but a simple statement of
proposal, suggesting that "dot" qualification MAY be used and that the
existing rules regarding placement of reference modification and/or
indexing/subscripting would remain in force, seems too simple for this
forum.

The approaching comet burns ever brighter in the sky, and I must get down to
the tar pit...<G>

Pete.

```
I had to look up the rule in the '85 Standard (I haven't looked in the
draft - but doubt that it is any different).  data-names (and MOST user
defined words) must have at least one alphabetic character (it need NOT be
the first one).  Procedure names (paragraph names and section names)
explicitly do NOT have that requirement - and may be all numeric.   I don't
think this was an "enhancement" in the '85 Standard, so my guess is that it
was also allowed in the '74 Standard - just no one "in their right mind"
ever did it. <G>.
```

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BB3BA34.4C1FBFDD@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<3b53a7e9$1@newsgate.hknet.com> <9j0lmv$7hv$1@fe2.cs.interbusiness.it> <m4r7ltgmu1rcd0tndapols0pqut2dudavj@4ax.com> <2s_47.22490$Kf3.292704@www.newsranger.com> <3B5482F7.17FC53AF@home.com> <3b555404_1@news2.newsgroups.com> <9j4cd7$cvv$1@mail.pl.unisys.com> <3b561f38_2@news2.newsgroups.com> <3B5EC2A9.279DC7A6@cusys.edu> <3B6897F4.DE32C2FB@istar.ca> <FSma7.15792$ar1.57837@www.newsranger.com> <3BB119A6.2BDB3A18@istar.ca> <npi5rtc2t38fv58t7h89rmqcj2ousmi2ts@4ax.com>`

```
The answer to the first question is that you can PERFORM or GO TO
paragraph-name of section-name.  The answer to the second is that
sometimes cosmetic changes can lead to greater use of a function. In
this case it also matches the syntax used by SQL, DYL280 (I think I've
seen it in Easytrieve) and C that I know of.  The current qualification
syntax is considered awkward and hard to read by many programmers who
have English as their first language.  Obviously I am one of thos who
dislikes it.  I think that it makes the subscipt or reference
modification clearer.  

Similarly cosmetics of a different sort may have doomed the MOVE and
ADD/SUBTRACT CORRESPONDING verbs.  By not having a compile option (no
standards involvement needed), that would cause the individual moves
(adds etc.) generated to be listed as comments after the CORRESPONDING
statement, any errors became difficult to find.  Another nice option
would have been a diagnostic that listed the mis-matches between the
sending and receiving structures.  If these were available you might not
see the antipathy toward the use of CORRESPONDING.

In conclusion, cosmetics can be important and influence the use of a
function.   

Volker Bandke wrote:
> 
> Clark,
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> Posted Via Binaries.net = SPEED+RETENTION+COMPLETION = http://www.binaries.net
```

Clark 

>The answer to the first question is that you can PERFORM or GO TO
>paragraph-name of section-name.

I must admit that I did not know this.  But, for my defense, I state:

I don't use Sections, unless I have to.  (when using declaratives,
e.g.).  And then, my sections still are not a conglomerate of several
more or less independent paragraphs, thus a GO TO or PERFORM  para-1
of section-2 is never needed

>The answer to the second is that
>sometimes cosmetic changes can lead to greater use of a function. 

<rest snipped>

May be, or may not be.  I cannot argue about this, but actually, I do
not really believe it.  In the context that COBOL is designed to be
"English Like", an instruction like

MOVE Last-name OF Customer-record TO Address-field-1

is more to my taste (and that what this is all about, taste) than

MOVE Customer-record.Last-name TO Address-field-1

De gustibus not est disputandum <s>

```
Volker Bandke wrote:
> 
> I don't use Sections, unless I have to.  (when using declaratives,
> e.g.).  And then, my sections still are not a conglomerate of several
> more or less independent paragraphs, thus a GO TO or PERFORM  para-1
> of section-2 is never needed

A more worrying issue with this is that it is not necessary to qualify
paragragh names, even if the name is duplicated.  It will silently use
the paragraph in the current section.

So if SECTION headers are used to loosely group paragraphs (as I myself
do) then an accidentally duplicated paragraph name can lead to annoying
problems.  The source code may appear that it should do one thing while
the actual code does another.
```

Just a little trivia that may have occurred earlier in this discussion. some
compilers (at least one) will in fact show the individual moves generated by
a CORRESPONDING. That would be the COBOL-74 compiler (ACOB) on Unisys
2200-series hardware. I'm not sure if the COBOL-85 compiler (UCOB) does the
same. Admittedly, a vendor-specific addition as opposed to a standard
feature.

> I have an application written with AcuCobol and I have necessity to import
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> What I can do?

Not a lot.

> The software has not been compiled with the option for ODBC!!

Putting the data into SQL Server will (most likely) not change where the
application looks for the data, it will still access the files that it
currently does.

It may be useful for _other_ purposes to have an extract of the data in
some database, but it would be necessary to extract the data using the
application, say reports 'printed' to disk files.  Then you would have
to pick out the data from the reports and load it into SQL with a
program, and do this on a regular basis to keep it up to date.
```

<a id="t-26aa7ce0ac"></a>
## Thread: Address Of Object in Working Storage — 2 msgs

### Address Of Object in Working Storage

- **From:** NotACobolGuru@att.net (Alex S)
- **Date:** 2001-09-26T16:54:25-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c9e25266.0109261554.601e70c@posting.google.com>`

```
My apologies if this has been answered before.  I did search these
groups and did not find an answer to my specific issue, so here it
is...

MVS/OS-390 Cobol II on ESA-390   (Yes, not current, but what we use.)

I know that IBM states clearly, and the compiler enforces, that I can
not take the address of an object in working storage, that I can only
take the address of a level 1 or level 77 object in linkage section. 
We do this routinely, however, using an assembler routine designed
just for that purpose.  I have also seen other answers in these groups
explaining how.

My question/problem, on the other hand, is why?  Is this "limitation"
an oversight, or is there a technical reason for the restriction?  I
suspect that the reason might be that objects in working storage can
"move around" dynamically and/or they might not be contiguous.  If
true, then taking the address of such objects is just plain wrong,
even if it seems to work.

We have lots of "production code" that does this.  It works.  I am
concerned that we may have laid a trap for ourselves, but I have been
unable to find any positive reason in the manuals for the restriction.
 Can someone help, please?  Thank you.
```

#### ↳ Re: Address Of Object in Working Storage

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-26T21:18:21-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9ou2au$4sf$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<c9e25266.0109261554.601e70c@posting.google.com>`

```
1) This restriction IS removed in the latest IBM compiler (IBM COBOL for
OS/390 & VM V2R2) - so no, there is no "technical/design" reason why it
shouldn't be allowed.

2) Doing this is REQUIRED to be supported in the draft of the next  COBOL
Standard. Therefore, assuming that IBM will "eventually" support the next
Standard, they will CONTINUE to include this support.

3)  Unless your shop is "heavy" into Assembler, I do recommend the "pass the
working-storage data item (not really an object) to a NESTED COBOL program -
and get the address there" technique - over an assembler subroutine to do
it.

4) The HISTORICAL reason that IBM did not (originally) allow this - when
they added support for ADDRESS OF syntax, was that it (by definition) would
stop some of their optimization.  If a linkage section item could be set to
an address (in a pointer data item)  that was also set to the address of a
Working-Storage item, this meant that you had "implicit" REDEFINES of items
in two different SECTIONS.  Once this is allowed, there is LOTS of
optimization that must be turned off.  (This same situation occurs whenever
a data item is passed BY REFERENCE in a CALL statement.  So the nested
program technique ALSO stops some optimization.)
```

## Thread: Too many client tasks (-1036) — 4 msgs

### Too many client tasks (-1036)

- **From:** "Stephen Plotnick" <thepla@attglobal.net>
- **Date:** 2001-09-27T11:17:22-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bb353d3_2@news1.prserv.net>`

```
I have an Access data base that has 7 tables in it. I am getting to the data
via ODBC and have been able to build the tables from input files
programmatically by deleting all the records and reading a sequential file
and inserting the records. I added a new table and get the above error on
the 64th record. I have deleted some input records to make sure it was not
the data and I still get the error on the 64th record. I can use the Access
"get external data" utility and have no problem getting all the data into
the Access table. Does anybody know what could be causing my problem? BTW,
after the data is in the table I am not having any problems fetching the
records.
```

Access 97 or Access 2000?

Running on a server or locally?

Check that you are not doing a CONNECT for each record... and not

some different values.

from this forum.

<a id="t-c8123935f7"></a>
## Thread: OO COBOL Class vs. Factory? — 8 this month / 11 total

_Topics: [`Object-oriented COBOL`](topics.md#oo)_

### OO COBOL Class vs. Factory?

- **From:** "Ahangar" <eahangar@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-27T19:50:52-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9p0dul0iv1@enews3.newsguy.com>`

```
What is the difference between a Class and a Factory in the proposed 200x
COBOL standards? Can someone please explain it with a short example?
Thanks
```

#### ↳ Re: OO COBOL Class vs. Factory?

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-28T17:57:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BB4BB3A.3D84D28D@home.com>`
- **References:** `<9p0dul0iv1@enews3.newsguy.com>`

```


Ahangar wrote:

> What is the difference between a Class and a Factory in the proposed 200x
> COBOL standards? Can someone please explain it with a short example?
> Thanks

There is no quick answer to this one. Actual OO syntax like "invoke this
method-name using xxxxx returning yyyyy" is no more difficult to comprehend
than "call thisprogram using xxx returning yyy".

Structuring classes, Factory methods and Object Section methods is the 'guts'
of what OO COBOL is all about. There are so many permutations that you can
make. For a very brief outline :-

    *----------------------
        Class-id.                        ThisClass inherits from Base.
        *--------------------
        FACTORY
        method1
        method2
        method etc...
        END FACTORY
        *---------
        OBJECT SECTION
        method10
        method11
        method....
        END OBJECT
    *----------------
    END ThisClass.
*-----------------------

Think of a Class as your source program - which is what it is. The above
would have a source name of "thisclass.cbl."  But classes are used to gather
together 'like' procedures to be performed on logical groups of objects :-

    Class - EditCustomers
    Class - CustomerFile
    Class - CustomerDialog

Each of the above classes is an OBJECT, that is, the runtime maintains an
object reference. Using an Animator I can see the object reference displayed
as an Hex value stored in memory. EditCustomers would invoke CustomerFile
(for file data, READ, WRITE, REWRITE) and  CustomerDalog (to both display
existing data and accept new or changed data). Much of this is achieved by
INHERITANCE :-

                    Base (the 'mother' of all Supers) and 'the buck stops
here'
                    ClassA inherits from Base  (Base is the Super for A)
                    ClassB inherits from ClassA (A is the Super for B)
                    ClassC inherits from ClassB etc.....

At the current time Fujitsu = FJBase, Hitachi = HB-Base and Micro Focus =
Base.

If I invoke ClassC "do-this-method" and that method-name appears in ClassA,
because of the Inheritance chain, it 'looks upward' to find the method in
Class A.
Note that this happens automatically without me having to do the equivalent
of non-OO :-

            call ClassA entry-point "do-this-paragraph" using xxx returning
yyy

You don't necessarily need all the pieces I show in the initial diagram -
depends upon what you want to do.

Non OO - call Myprogram using these parameters
OO         - invoke MyProgram "method-name" using these parameters

Both function in the same way, continuing until you signal "'EXIT" which for
PCs would take us back to the application's Main Menu.

Factory - the key here is a 'Factory' is used to MANUFACTURE (process) or
BUILD objects. Sometimes you need a "new" object, sometimes you don't :-

- Single User - wanting EditCustomers - we don't need to differentiate
between Factory and Object Section so we invoke, say from a Main Menu :-

    invoke EditCustomers "begin" using Desktop

Here all the methods we are gong to use can be in Factory.

Using Desktop gives us a reference for Windows to display any dialogs Think
of "begin" as the Main-Line in a non-OO program - the starting point. I could
just as easily have used the method name "main-line" as an alternative to
"begin".

- Multi User - here we have 10 people using EditCustomers - so now Factory as
a 'controller' comes into play :-

    for User1 invoke CustomersEdit "new" returning os-CustomerEdit1
    for User2 invoke CustomersEdit "new" returning os-CustomerEdit2
    for User3 etc....

You don't "actually' return 1, 2 and 3 etc  as I've shown, but a reference
equivalent of 1, 2 or 3 to each user's PC.

Class : CustomersEdit
*>-------------------
FACTORY
method-id. "new".
linkage section.
01 lnk-self                    object reference.
Procedure Division returning lnk-self.
  invoke super "new" returning lnk-self    *> Super = Base
End Method "new".
End Factory
Object.
Method-id "begin".
  invoke Method2
  invoke Method3
  invoke etc....
Method2.
Method3
End Object..
End Class CustomerEdit
*--------------------------

Where I have "...returning os-CustomerEdit1, os-CustomerEdit2....." above,
each has an OBJECT REFERENCE or HANDLE.

Having got that handle now each user has :-

    invoke os-CustomerEdit "begin" using the methods in the OBJECT SECTION

Factory contains the method "new" and this creates an INSTANCE of the
class(program) as it will be used by say User #9. This is the bit which is a
little tricky to put across. Those 3 classes I started out with - times 10
users equals 30 programs. Right ? Wrong !  Only 3 - one copy of each. Even
though we have 10 users the INSTANTIATION allows each to reference common
methods but for each User there is a separate copy for their specific data,
held in Global Working Storage..

If that seems weird, you have to bear in mind that all methods are
transitory. You invoke methods in different ways :-

    StartProgram -  invoke ProgramC "do-this" using xxx returning yyyy
    ProgramC - invoke self "do-this" using xxx returning yyyy
    ProgramC - invoke super (ProgramB) "do-this-one" using xxx returning yyyy

*                    here I'm telling C to go to Super (B) to action a
specific method

When you have finished with a method - "End method",  the local-storage
variables in that method are 'gone'; that's not the case if you move values
into Global Working Storage.
-----------------------------------------------------------------------------

It's one hellava topic to try and paraphrase. If you are intrigued by OO, go
to cobolreport.com, a reasonable number of articles in their archives and you
could order a copy of Will Price's book "Elements of OO".

To summarize - if you have a firm grasp of non-OO COBOL you are 90% of the
way there on coding for OO. Literally, you have to let your imagination fly
on all the twists and turns you can do in OO to give you very, very great
flexibility - and that comes about using the hackneyed word REUSABILITY -
where you are constantly searching where you can re-use code, by segregating
into invokable classes, rather than keep on "copy and paste', "copy and
paste'...
.
Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

#### ↳ Re: OO COBOL Class vs. Factory?

- **From:** Thane Hubbell <nospam@newsranger.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-28T21:52:43+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Li6t7.8806$ev2.16173@www.newsranger.com>`
- **References:** `<9p0dul0iv1@enews3.newsguy.com>`

```
The factory is part of the class.  Every class has a single factory object.  You
can have multiple instance objects, but only a single factory object of a class
exists.  This is the "constructor" as it were.  The home of the "new" method.
Any data that you want to exist for all instances of a class should be defined
in the factory and accessed via factory methods.  

To reference a factory method or property you specify the class name for the
object reference.


In article <9p0dul0iv1@enews3.newsguy.com>, Ahangar says...
>
>What is the difference between a Class and a Factory in the proposed 200x
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OO COBOL Class vs. Factory?

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-29T02:01:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3BB52CC1.1A9EAC00@home.com>`
- **References:** `<9p0dul0iv1@enews3.newsguy.com> <Li6t7.8806$ev2.16173@www.newsranger.com> <9p2vi1018ul@enews3.newsguy.com>`

```


Ahangar wrote:

> Thanks for the explanation. My exposure to OO COBOL has been with Micro
> Focus only, and they have not implemented the Factory concept yet. So is it
> safe to say that Factory in the proposed standard is equivalent of
> Class-Object in current Micro Focus implementation?

Would have helped if you had said you were using M/F OO <G>.

Yes. No wonder your confusion. But the reasoning is that M/F started out on an
early version of OO, and called it Class-Object way before the Standard's people
decided to call it FACTORY in the proposed COBOL 2002. Another one you should
bear in mind, our Object-Storage Section in M/F becomes Working-Storage Section
in COBOL 2002.

Seeing the word 'Object' popping up all over the place in Net Express confused
the hell out of me - so from day one I used only the syntax FACTORY.

Jimmy
```

Dear readers,

we too have a problem similar to the one poerschke@my-dejanews.com
reported on 1998/07/23 to this group. We are using NetExpress 3.1 and
MSVC 6.0 under W2K. After loading a
COBOL created dll the locale settings are all set to the system
default
which is bad, as our C++ Program expects "C" as locale:

printf(setlocale(LC_ALL, NULL)); // "C" is returned
dllhandle = LoadLibrary("CBLRTSS.dll"); // or some dll we have build

FreeLibrary(dllhandle);
printf(setlocale(LC_ALL, NULL)); // "German_german.1251" or something
alike is returned

insights today?

from time to time and some folks are still wearing muttonchop sideburns.

Stefan,

from your name I ASSume that you are German, and thus are familiar
with the "Nuernberger Trichter" - Let me assure you, there is no such
thing, not even for COBOL.  Relatively Quick - Relatively Easy ...
These terms are somewhat .... relative ???  DO you expect to master a
language in 1 year?  And if you know it after 11 Months, was this
relatively quick?


A _good_ starting point is Thane Hubbels excellent book:  

Teach Yourself COBOL in 24 hours.  
ISBN: 0-672-31453-3

I can really recommend it (And no, I don't get any commission.  I do
use his book though in all the COBOL classes I teach, and I know it is
a very good item to have on your shelf.  Also, don't believe the 24
hours stuff, like start working on this book on Monday morning, and to
know COBOL by Tuesday)

After you have worked through Thane's book (which contains a compiler
for COBOL that runs on your windows PC), you might want to get 

COBOL Unleashed
ISBN: 0-672-31254-9

and also

Advanced COBOL
ISBN: 0-471-31481-1

<a id="t-9f9af9df7a"></a>
## Thread: strange output — 4 this month / 9 total

### strange output

- **From:** pauldbq@yahoo.com (dbq)

```
I'm getting some strange output when I run MF Cobol.  I have Animator
2. V.8.2.75 on Windows.  Let me preface this by saying I think that I
have a compiler directive set that I can't set back to the default. 
So my first question is how do you set all the compiler directives
back to their default values?

That said here is the output:

  EMPLOYEE       EMPLOYEE            OFF  MONTHLY SOC. SEC. # OF JOB
   NUMBER          NAME              NUMB SALARY   NUMBER   DEP. CODE

   NU00001  SMITH                    N01B   500Y 123-45-6789D01. 23DE
   NU00002  JONES                    N01B  1000Y 987-65-4321D09. 87DE
            PROGRAM: REV7_IN.DAT                 OUT:  REV7_OUT.DOC


As you can see, the ouput has alphanumeric values that should not be
there.  My input file is as follows:

00001SMITH               010105000012345678901234567890123456789012345
00002JONES               020110000098765432109876543210987654321098765

Note that the input file is a *.DAT file and the output file is a .DOC
file.

So, I suspect I set a compiler directive that is causing this.

Finally, the real issue is the monthly salary field.  If I read and
write it as a PIC X(6) field it is fine, but if I attempt to change
the field to
PIC 9(4)V99 (both read and write), the program fails.

The error message I get is 'RTS0163 Runtime Error, Illegal Character
in Numberic Field (error 163)'.

The line the error message is highlighting is:

           MOVE MONTHLY-SALARY-IN TO MONTHLY-SALARY-OUT

The section of code that is relevant is as follows:


       FILE SECTION.
       FD  PAYROLL-FILE LABEL RECORDS ARE STANDARD.
       01  PAYROLL-REC.
           05  EMPLOYEE-NUMBER-IN                PIC X(5).
           05  EMPLOYEE-NAME-IN                  PIC X(20).
           05  LOCATION-CODE-IN.
               10  TERRITORY-NUMBER-IN           PIC XX.
               10  OFFICE-NUMBER-IN              PIC XX.
           05  MONTHLY-SALARY-IN                 PIC 9(4)V99.
           05  SOCIAL-SECURITY-NUMBER-IN         PIC X(9).
           05  NUMBER-OF-DEPENDENTS-IN           PIC XX.
           05  JOB-CLASSIFICATION-CODE-IN        PIC XX.
           05  UNUSED-IN                         PIC X(22).
       FD  PRINT-FILE LABEL RECORDS ARE STANDARD.
       01  PRINT-REC.
           05                                  PIC X(5).
           05  EMPLOYEE-NUMBER-OUT             PIC X(5) VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC XX.
           05  EMPLOYEE-NAME-OUT               PIC X(20) VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC X.
           05  TERRITORY-NUMBER-OUT            PIC XX VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC X(3).
           05  OFFICE-NUMBER-OUT               PIC XX VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC XX.
           05  MONTHLY-SALARY-OUT              PIC 9(4)V99 VALUE
ZEROS.
           05                                  PIC XX.
           05  SOCIAL-SECURITY-NUMBER-OUT      PIC X(9) VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC XX.
           05  NUMBER-OF-DEPENDENTS-OUT        PIC XX VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC XX.
           05  JOB-CLASSIFICATION-CODE-OUT     PIC XX VALUE SPACES.
           05                                  PIC X(3).

In summary the problem I am running into is having the compiler
recognize that one particular field as a NUMERIC field as opposed to a
ALPHANUMERIC field.
```

On 30 Sep 2001 10:47:56 -0700 pauldbq@yahoo.com (dbq) wrote:

Not a clue.

:>That said here is the output:

:>  EMPLOYEE       EMPLOYEE            OFF  MONTHLY SOC. SEC. # OF JOB
:>   NUMBER          NAME              NUMB SALARY   NUMBER   DEP. CODE

:>   NU00001  SMITH                    N01B   500Y 123-45-6789D01. 23DE
:>   NU00002  JONES                    N01B  1000Y 987-65-4321D09. 87DE
:>            PROGRAM: REV7_IN.DAT                 OUT:  REV7_OUT.DOC


:>As you can see, the ouput has alphanumeric values that should not be
:>there.  My input file is as follows:

If I recall correctly, the VALUE clause in the FILE SECTION (or LINKAGE
SECTION), when allowed, is merely a comment. The record area in the FILE
SECTION is not really part of your data area, it is a system area mapped to
your program. 

Most programmers would recommend having the detailed output record area in the
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION and just an 01 level in the file section, and to use
WRITE FROM.

:>00001SMITH               010105000012345678901234567890123456789012345
:>00002JONES               020110000098765432109876543210987654321098765

:>Note that the input file is a *.DAT file and the output file is a .DOC
:>file.

:>So, I suspect I set a compiler directive that is causing this.

:>Finally, the real issue is the monthly salary field.  If I read and
:>write it as a PIC X(6) field it is fine, but if I attempt to change
:>the field to
:>PIC 9(4)V99 (both read and write), the program fails.

I don't understand how the trailing zeroes are being suppressed while using
either PIC 9(4)V99 or X(6).

:>The error message I get is 'RTS0163 Runtime Error, Illegal Character
:>in Numberic Field (error 163)'.

:>The line the error message is highlighting is:

I would suggest DISPLAYing PAYROLL-REC or: 

             05  MONTHLY-SALARY-IN-CHAR.
                 10  MONTHLY-SALARY-IN             PIC 9(4)V99.

and DISPLAYing MONTHLY-SALARY-IN-CHAR.


:>           MOVE MONTHLY-SALARY-IN TO MONTHLY-SALARY-OUT

:>The section of code that is relevant is as follows:

I suggest posting the entire program rather than your abstract of the DATA
DIVISION.

   [ snipped ]
```

#### ↳ Re: strange output

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-09-30T16:55:15-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9p84cm$r2k$1@slb1.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<b7bceb3e.0109300947.1ff4c76a@posting.google.com>`

```
As other have already stated,

A) you probably want a numeric-edited rather than numeric field in your
output (print) file, i.e. "." rather than "V" in the PICTURE.

B) VALUE clauses (other than for 88-levels) are ignored in the File section
(when allowed as an extension to the ANSI Standard).

  ***

However, I don't think that EITHER of those would cause the problem that you
are getting.

From your record descriptions and the input data that you show us, I can
think of two possibilities - neither of them real likely - but still
possible.

1) Do you define your input (and probably output) files as "LINE SEQUENTIAL"
in the Select/Assign clause?  If the input file is "delimited" by
carriage-return and line-feed (i.e. a "normal" PC or Unix file) then this
could cause you to get "odd" results and to seem to get non-numeric data in
numeric input fields.

2) When you run the program under Animator, what *IS* the value of
    MONTHLY-SALARY-IN
just before the "problem"?   Is there some chance that you are trying to
"process" a record *after* end-of-file, (i.e. working on a "3rd" record when
you only have 2).  This would cause you to process a "messy buffer" of data
without having a numeric value in in your input field.

 ***

There are run-time (and compiler) features to "get around" 163 errors (with
Micro Focus) - particularly the +/-F switch.  HOWEVER, I don't think you
should "play with that" as you seem to be having some other problem.

HINT:  In the "real world" it is usually a VERY GOOD idea to check numeric
fields with the "IF NUMERIC" test before trying to use them as numeric data.
My guess is that you are very early in your COBOL training, but you will
soon be required to deal with "invalid" input.
```
